The Two Testimonies: Foundation of Complete Faith
Ghazali enumerates the superiority of the Prophet and the conditions for complete faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that complete faith requires both the testimony of tawhid and the testimony of the Messenger, and that belief in everything the Prophet conveyed is obligatory.

Core Idea
Faith is incomplete without testifying to both Allah's oneness and Muhammad's prophethood.

Imam al-Ghazali begins this section by highlighting the unique status of Prophet Muhammad and the inseparable link between the two testimonies. This passage establishes that true faith is not merely a verbal declaration of tawhid but must be joined with acceptance of Muhammad as Allah's Messenger, along with belief in all he taught.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the Prophet's Superiority
Notice that the passage first affirms that Allah made Prophet Muhammad superior to all other prophets and the master of mankind. This sets the stage for the obligation to follow him.
This emphasizes his unique rank.
2
Understand the Necessity of Both Testimonies
Read the part about the testimony of tawhid being insufficient without the testimony of the Messenger. Understand that 'La ilaha illa Allah' must be accompanied by 'Muhammad Rasul Allah' for faith to be complete.
This is a core doctrinal point.
3
Grasp the Obligation to Believe All He Informed
The passage then mandates belief in everything the Prophet conveyed about this world and the Hereafter, including after death. Reflect on why this is necessary for true faith.
Faith is not selective; it must be comprehensive.

سائر الأنبياء (١)، وجعله سيد البشر (۲) ، ومنع كمال الإيمان بشهادة التوحيد ، وهو قول لا إله إلا الله ما لم تقترن بها شهادة الرسول وهو قولك ، محمد رسول الله (۳) وألزم الخلق تصديقه في جميع ما أخبر عنه من أمور الدنيا والآخر (4) ، وأنه لا يتقبل إيمان عبد حتى يؤمن بما أخبر به بعد الموت (٥) ؛
Among all the prophets (1), and He made him the master of mankind (2), and He prevented the completeness of faith by the testimony of tawhid, which is the statement 'There is no god but Allah' unless it is accompanied by the testimony of the Messenger, which is your statement 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' (3), and He obligated creation to believe him in everything he informed of concerning matters of this world and the Hereafter (4), and that He does not accept the faith of a servant until he believes in what he informed of after death (5);
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the inseparable nature of the two testimonies for complete faith. It begins by affirming the superiority of Prophet Muhammad over all prophets, stating that Allah made him the master of mankind. Then it establishes that the testimony of tawhid ('La ilaha illa Allah') alone is insufficient for complete faith unless it is joined by the testimony of the Messenger ('Muhammad Rasul Allah'). The passage goes on to mandate belief in everything the Prophet conveyed regarding worldly and otherworldly matters, emphasizing that no servant's faith is accepted until they believe in what he informed about after death—referring to events of the grave and the afterlife. The numbers in parentheses likely refer to footnotes that expand on each point, but the main text asserts these core doctrinal obligations.

The Limits of Analogy and Avoiding Anthropomorphism
Ghazali explains analogical knowledge of God and refutes anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses analogical reasoning to attribute perfect attributes to God while cautioning that shared terms do not imply similarity or anthropomorphism.

Core Idea
Shared attributes do not entail similarity; God's attributes are incomparably more perfect.

Imam al-Ghazali begins by explaining a fundamental principle of human cognition: we only know ourselves directly, and from that we infer knowledge of others by analogy. When we apply this to God, we must affirm the same categories of attributes (power, knowledge, will) but with the conviction that His are infinitely superior. However, a major pitfall is to assume that sharing a name or concept means God resembles creation. Ghazali uses the example of black and white to illustrate that even opposites can share many attributes without being similar. This lesson establishes the proper method of affirming divine attributes without falling into tashbih (anthropomorphism).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the analogical method
Notice how Ghazali says man perceives himself and then reasons by analogy to others, including God. What does he say about affirming attributes for God?
Pay attention to the phrase 'ثم بالمقايسة إليه يفهم ذلك لغيره'.
2
Examine the clarification about shared attributes
Ghazali warns against thinking that sharing an attribute implies similarity. Follow his example of black and white sharing the property of being an accident. How does this relate to God's attributes?
3
Reflect on the Prophet's saying
The Prophet's statement 'I cannot enumerate praise upon You' is cited as a conclusion of this line of reasoning. Consider how it fits.
4
Summarise the key rule
Formulate in your own words the principle that sharing an attribute with God does not make one similar to Him in essence.
This principle is central to Islamic theology about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة فلا يدرك الإنسان إلا نفسه وصفات نفسه مما هي حاضرة له في الحال أو مما كانت له من قبل ، ثم بالمقايسة إليه يفهم ذلك لغيره ، ثم قد يصدق بأن بينهما تفاوتا في الشرف والكمال ، فليس في قوة البشر إلا أن يثبت الله تعالى ما هو ثابت لنفسه من الفعل والعلم والقدرة وغيرها (۱) من الصفات مع التصديق بأن ذلك أكمل وأشرف ، فيكون معظم تحويمه على صفات نفسه لا على ما اختص الرب تعالى به من الجلال (۲) ولذلك قال صلى الله عليه وسلم .
لا أحصي ثناء عليك أنت كما أثنيت على نفسك (۳)
(۱) وفي نسخه أخرى : من الفعل والعلم والقدرة وغيره من الصفات
والمراد من قوله من الصفات : الصفات التي يتوهم فيها الإشتراك.
(۲) ولا ينبغي أن يظن أن المشاركة بكل وصف توجب المماثلة ، ذلك أن الضدين يتماثلان وبينهما غاية البعد الذي لا يتصور أن يكون بعد فوقه ، وهما متشاركان في أوصاف كثيرة إذا السواد يشارك البياض في كونه عرضا وفي كونه مدركا بالبصر وأمور أخر سواه ، أفتراى من قال : إن الله تعالى موجود لا في محل ، وأنه سميع بصير عالم مريد متكلم حي قادر فاعل ، وللإنسان أيضاً كذلك ، فقد شبه قائل هذا إذ لا أقل من إثبات المشاركة في الوجود وهو موهم للمشابهة بل المماثلة عبارة عن المشاركة في النوع والماهية ، فيكون العبد رحيما صبوراً شكوراً ، لا يوجب المماثلة ولا لكونه سميعاً بصيراً عالماً قادراً حياً فاعلاً.
(۳) أخرجه الإمام مسلم في صحيحه من حديث عائشة رضي الله عنها ، أنها سمعت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول ذلك في سنجوده
وفي رواية أخرى كما أخرجه الإمام مسلم في صحيحه أيضاً بسنده عن أبي هريرة عن عائشة رضي الله عنها قالت : فقدت رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ذات ليلة من الفراش فالتمسته فوقعت يدي على بطن قدميه وهو في المسجد وهما منصوبتان وهو يقول : اللهم إني أعوذ برضاك من سخطك ، وبمعناك من عقوبتك ، وأعوذ بك منك لا أحصي ثناء عليك أنت كما أثنيت على نفسك . وهذا الحديث أخرجه الإمام أحمد أيضا عن أبي أسامة ، ثم قال الحافظ ابن حجر العسقلاني في تخريج أحاديث الأذكار : ( وفي السند لطيفة : وهي رواية صحابي عن صحابي : أبو هريرة عن عائشة
وليس المعنى أني أعجز عن التعبير عما أدركته ، بل هو اعتراف بالقصور عن إدراك جلاله (۱) ولذلك قال بعضهم (۲) : ما عرف الله بالحقيقة سوى الله عز وجل .
وقال الصديق رضي الله عنه : الحمد لله الذي لم يجعل للخلق سبيلا إلى معرفته إلا بالعجز عن معرفته (۳)
(۱) يقول أبو حامد الغزالي رضي الله عنه في كتابه النفيس و المقصد الأسني »
ولم يرد به أنه عرف منه ما لا يطاوعه لسانه في العبارة عنه بل معناه إني لا أحيط بمحامدك وصفات إلهيتك وإنما أنت المحيط بها وحدك ، فإذا لا يحيط مخلوق من ملاحظة حقيقة ذاته إلا بالحيرة والدهشة ، وأما إتساع المعرفة فإنما يكون في معرفة أسمائه وصفاته » اهـ
(۲) وهو أبو القاسم الجنيد رحمه الله تعالى ورضي عنه .
(۳) يقول الإمام الغزالي رضي الله عنه
نهاية معرفة العارفين عجزهم عن المعرفة ، ومعرفتهم بالحقيقة هي أنهم لا يعرفونه وأنه لا يمكنهم معرفته وأنه يستحيل أن يعرف الله المعرفة الحقيقية المحيطة بكنه صفات الربوبية الا الله تعالى ، فإذا إنكشف لهم ذلك إنكشافاً برهانياً فقد عرفوه » .
In short, man perceives only himself and his own attributes—those present to him at the moment or those he had before. Then by analogy to himself he understands that for others. Then he may affirm that between them there is a disparity in honor and perfection. So the power of humans is only to affirm for God, may He be exalted, what is affirmed for themselves of action, knowledge, power, and other attributes, while affirming that this is more perfect and more honorable. Thus most of his focus is on his own attributes, not on what is particular to the Lord, may He be exalted, of majesty. For that reason, the Prophet, peace be upon him, said: 'I cannot enumerate praise upon You; You are as You have praised Yourself.'
(1) And in another copy: of action, knowledge, power, and other attributes. And the meaning of His saying 'of attributes' is the attributes in which sharing is imagined.
(2) And it should not be thought that sharing in every attribute entails similarity. For two opposites are similar? and between them is the utmost distance beyond which no distance can be imagined, yet they share many attributes—indeed black shares with white in being an accident and in being perceived by sight and other things besides. Do you not see that he who says: God exists not in a place, and that He is hearing, seeing, knowing, willing, speaking, living, powerful, acting, and man also is like that—such a speaker has committed anthropomorphism (tashbih)? For there is at least the affirmation of sharing in existence, which suggests similarity. Rather, similarity (tamathul) is an expression of sharing in type and essence. So if the servant is merciful, patient, grateful, this does not entail similarity, nor does his being hearing, seeing, knowing, powerful, living, acting entail it.
(3) Narrated by Imam Muslim in his Sahih from the hadith of Aisha, may God be pleased with her, that she heard the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, say that in his prostration. In another narration, also as narrated by Imam Muslim in his Sahih with his chain from Abu Huraira from Aisha, may God be pleased with her, she said: I missed the Messenger of God, peace be upon him, one night from the bed, so I sought him and my hand fell on the soles of his feet while he was in the mosque and they were raised, and he was saying: 'O God, I seek refuge in Your pleasure from Your wrath, and in Your pardon from Your punishment, and I seek refuge in You from You. I cannot enumerate praise upon You; You are as You have praised Yourself.' This hadith was also narrated by Imam Ahmad from Abu Usama. Then al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar al-Asqalani said in Takhrij Ahadith al-Adhkar: 'In the chain is a subtle point: it is the narration of a Companion from a Companion: Abu Huraira from Aisha.'
And the meaning is not that I am incapable of expressing what I have perceived, but rather it is an acknowledgment of deficiency in comprehending His majesty. (1) Therefore some said: (2) 'None knows God truly except God, mighty and majestic.' And al-Siddiq (Abu Bakr), may God be pleased with him, said: 'Praise be to God who has not given creation a way to know Him except by the inability to know Him.' (3)
(1) Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, may God be pleased with him, says in his precious book al-Maqsad al-Asna: 'He did not intend by this that he knows from Him what his tongue cannot express; rather its meaning is: I do not encompass Your praises and Your divine attributes, and only You encompass them alone. So no creature can contemplate the reality of His essence except with bewilderment and astonishment. As for the expansion of knowledge, it is only in knowledge of His names and attributes.' End quote.
(2) He is Abu al-Qasim al-Junayd, may God have mercy on him and be pleased with him.
(3) Imam al-Ghazali, may God be pleased with him, says: 'The ultimate knowledge of the knowers is their inability to know, and their true knowledge is that they do not know Him and cannot know Him, and that it is impossible for anyone to know God with true knowledge that encompasses the inner reality of the attributes of Lordship except God. So when this is unveiled to them with demonstrative unveiling, they have known Him.'
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the limits of human knowledge of God and the proper meaning of praising Him. Ghazali begins by stating that man only perceives himself and his own attributes, then by analogy understands that others have similar attributes. However, when applying this to God, one must affirm the same categories (action, knowledge, power, etc.) while acknowledging that God's attributes are far more perfect and honorable. The consequence is that a person's focus remains on his own attributes, not on the unique majesty of God. This leads to the Prophet's saying, 'I cannot enumerate praise upon You; You are as You have praised Yourself.'

Then a footnote clarifies that the attributes in question are those where shared terms might be imagined. Ghazali then makes a key clarification: having a shared attribute does not imply similarity (tamathul) between God and creation. He gives the example of opposites like black and white, which share many properties (being accidents, perceived by sight) yet are utterly different in kind. The one who affirms existence for God and also for man commits anthropomorphism (tashbih) because at minimum existence is shared, suggesting similarity. True similarity (tamathul) means sharing in species and essence, which does not occur between God and creatures. Thus a servant being merciful, patient, etc., does not entail similarity to God's attributes.

The hadith is then cited with multiple chains, including the subtle point that Abu Huraira narrated from Aisha (a Companion from a Companion).

On page 122, Ghazali explains the meaning of the hadith: it is not that the Prophet was unable to express what he perceived, but rather it is an acknowledgment of inability to comprehend God's majesty itself. He quotes al-Junayd: 'None knows God truly except God.' And Abu Bakr al-Siddiq: 'Praise be to God who has not given creation a way to know Him except by the inability to know Him.'

Finally, a footnote from Ghazali's own al-Maqsad al-Asna expands: the Prophet did not mean he could not express his knowledge, but that he cannot encompass God's praises. True knowledge of God leads to bewilderment at His essence, while expansion of knowledge occurs in knowing His names and attributes. Another footnote from Ghazali states that the ultimate knowledge of the gnostics is their inability to know, and that when this is revealed to them demonstratively, they have truly known Him.

The passage thus establishes a via negativa approach to knowledge of God's essence while affirming that we can know His attributes in a way that avoids anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
المشاركة (sharing)
المماثلة (similarity)
التشبيه (anthropomorphism)
المقايسة (analogy)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any shared attribute between God and creation implies similarity or anthropomorphism.
Correction: Sharing an attribute like existence or knowledge does not entail similarity; similarity requires sharing in species and essence, which is absent between God and creation.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does sharing an attribute between God and creation entail?
A) It implies similarity.
B) It does not imply similarity.
C) It implies anthropomorphism.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Shared attribute names between God and creation do not indicate resemblance; true similarity requires sharing in species and essence, which does not occur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you describe God as 'Merciful', do you secretly imagine a human-like mercy? How does Ghazali's distinction between sharing attributes and similarity challenge your own mental image of God? What will you do to purify your conception?
?
The Prophet said, 'I cannot enumerate praise upon You; You are as You have praised Yourself.' Have you ever felt that your own words of praise fall short? In what specific way does this lesson correct your tendency to measure God by your own standards?
?
You often think of God as 'knowing' like you know things, only more. After reading Ghazali's argument, what attribute of God do you most need to remind yourself is incomparable? How will you actively avoid reducing God to your own experience?

Divine Transcendence
intermediate
4 min
The Meaning of the Prophet's Praise: Acknowledging Inability to Comprehend God
Ghazali interprets the Prophet's praise as an acknowledgment of limited comprehension.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Prophet's statement 'I cannot enumerate praise upon You' is an acknowledgment of the impossibility of fully comprehending God's majestic essence, not an inability to articulate praise.

Core Idea
True praise of God lies in recognizing the limits of human comprehension.

In the previous lesson, we saw how Ghazali established the proper method of attributing perfections to God while avoiding anthropomorphism. Now we delve into the deeper meaning of the Prophet's own words of praise: 'I cannot enumerate praise upon You; You are as You have praised Yourself.' Ghazali emphatically clarifies that this is not a confession of expressive failure, but a profound recognition that the human intellect cannot encompass the reality of God's majesty. He supports this with the statements of al-Junayd and Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, culminating in the famous principle that the highest knowledge of God is the knowledge of one's inability to know Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the original hadith and Ghazali's interpretation
Notice how Ghazali first refutes the misunderstanding that the Prophet meant inability to express what he perceived.
2
Understand the concept of 'inability to comprehend'
Ghazali explains that the hadith is an acknowledgment of deficiency in comprehending God's majesty, not in expressing known truths.
3
Examine the supporting quotes from Junayd and Abu Bakr
These quotes reinforce that only God fully knows Himself, and that true knowledge of God is realized through the awareness of one's ignorance.
4
Reflect on Ghazali's further explanation in al-Maqsad al-Asna
This expands on the distinction between knowing God's essence (impossible) and knowing His names and attributes (possible).

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument about the limits of human knowledge of God and the proper meaning of praising Him. Ghazali begins by stating that man only perceives himself and his own attributes, then by analogy understands that others have similar attributes. However, when applying this to God, one must affirm the same categories (action, knowledge, power, etc.) while acknowledging that God's attributes are far more perfect and honorable. The consequence is that a person's focus remains on his own attributes, not on the unique majesty of God. This leads to the Prophet's saying, 'I cannot enumerate praise upon You; You are as You have praised Yourself.'

Then a footnote clarifies that the attributes in question are those where shared terms might be imagined. Ghazali then makes a key clarification: having a shared attribute does not imply similarity (tamathul) between God and creation. He gives the example of opposites like black and white, which share many properties (being accidents, perceived by sight) yet are utterly different in kind. True similarity (tamathul) means sharing in species and essence, which does not occur between God and creatures. Thus a servant being merciful, patient, etc., does not entail similarity to God's attributes.

The hadith is then cited with multiple chains, including the subtle point that Abu Huraira narrated from Aisha (a Companion from a Companion).

On page 122, Ghazali explains the meaning of the hadith: it is not that the Prophet was unable to express what he perceived, but rather it is an acknowledgment of inability to comprehend God's majesty itself. He quotes al-Junayd: 'None knows God truly except God.' And Abu Bakr al-Siddiq: 'Praise be to God who has not given creation a way to know Him except by the inability to know Him.'

Another footnote from Ghazali states that the ultimate knowledge of the gnostics is their inability to know, and that when this is revealed to them demonstratively, they have truly known Him.

The passage thus establishes a via negativa approach to knowledge of God's essence while affirming that we can know His attributes in a way that avoids anthropomorphism.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Prophet was confessing an inability to express praise.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that it is an acknowledgment of the impossibility of fully comprehending God's majesty, not a failure of expression.

Checkpoint

D) He was being humble despite knowing everything.

Key Takeaway
The highest knowledge of God is the recognition that His essence is beyond full human comprehension, and true praise begins with this humble acknowledgment.

Things Beyond Intellect: Soul and Divine Attributes
Ghazali introduces a category of things beyond intellectual grasp with two examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ghazali identifies a category of things—including the soul and some of God's attributes—that human intellect cannot fully grasp.

Core Idea
The soul and God's attributes mark the limits of discursive reason.

Ghazali turns away from his discussion on praise to return to his main purpose. He reveals that among the divisions of knowable things, there are matters that exhaust the intellect's ability to perceive them. Two prime examples are the human soul (al-ruh) and certain attributes of Allah. This sets the stage for a deeper appreciation of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the transition
Begin by reading the opening line where Ghazali steers the discussion back to his main subject. Notice how he signals a shift in direction.
The phrase 'turn the reins of speech' is a vivid metaphor for redirecting discourse.
2
Identify the classification
Ghazali states he finds among 'the divisions' things that minds tire from. Understand that he is asserting a category of objects that surpass rational comprehension.
The term 'divisions' (al-aqsam) likely refers to categories of existent or knowable things.
3
Focus on the two examples
Look for the two examples Ghazali gives: the spirit (al-ruh) and some of God's attributes. Ask yourself why these are particularly fitting examples.
The soul is enigmatic: Quran 17:85 says 'the spirit is of the command of my Lord.'
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why some divine attributes would be beyond intellectual grasp. This anticipation connects to the broader theme of tanzih (transcendence) in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنقيض عنان الكلام عن هذا النمط ، ولنرجع الى الغرض وهو أن أجد الأقسام ما تكل الأفهام عن إدراكه ، ومن جملته الروح ، ومن جملته بعض صفات الله تعالى

Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is a methodological transition and a concluding illustration of the limits of human intellect. Ghazali turns away from the preceding discussion about praise and knowledge and returns to his main purpose (al-gharad). He states that among the 'divisions' (presumably of things that can be known) there are matters that exhaust the intellect's ability to perceive them. He gives two examples: the spirit (al-ruh) and some of God's attributes. The reference to the soul echoes the Qur'anic verse that 'the spirit is of the command of my Lord' (17:85), indicating its mysterious nature. Then he suggests that a similar indication is found in the prophetic saying: 'God has seventy veils of light; if He were to remove them, the glories of His face would burn everything that His sight perceives.' This hadith illustrates that direct perception of God's majesty is overwhelming and beyond human capacity. A footnote from al-Hafiz al-Iraqi notes that the hadith is transmitted by Abu al-Shaykh ibn Hibban in Kitab al-Azama from Abu Huraira, but its chain is weak. The text ends abruptly with 'and also in...' indicating a citation that continues elsewhere. The overall point is to ground the earlier discussion in a concrete example: just as the soul is beyond full comprehension, so are certain divine attributes, underscoring the theme that true knowledge of God recognizes its own limits.

Correction: Ghazali means that these matters are inherently beyond the discursive reasoning process—they are not just difficult but ultimately exceed the domain of rational demonstration, pointing to a realm that must be accepted through revelation and spiritual insight.

Seventy Veils: Illustration of Divine Transcendence
Ghazali cites a prophetic hadith to illustrate the overwhelming nature of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses a prophetic hadith about seventy veils of light to illustrate the overwhelming nature of God's glory and the limits of creation's perception.

Core Idea
God's glory is so immense it is veiled; if unveiled, it would burn all who see.

After establishing that some matters are beyond intellect, Ghazali provides a vivid illustration from prophetic tradition. He cites a hadith: Allah has seventy veils of light; if removed, the glories of His face would burn everything that perceives them. This hadith powerfully depicts divine transcendence and the protective purpose of the veils. A footnote notes the hadith's weak chain, but Ghazali's use remains theological illustration rather than proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith carefully
Read the sentence beginning 'Perhaps the indication...' and focus on the imagery: seventy veils of light, removal, burning of everything in sight.
The number 'seventy' in Arabic often denotes multiplicity, not an exact count.
2
Interpret the veil imagery
Consider what the veils represent. In Islamic thought, veils (hijab) can symbolize divine protection, majesty, or the barrier between God and creation.
Light (nur) is a symbol of God's presence and knowledge.
3
Consider the weak chain note
The footnote says the hadith's chain is weak. Why might Ghazali still use it? He is not establishing doctrine but offering an illustration of a theological principle.
Many scholars use weak hadith for motivational or illustrative purposes, not for creedal proofs.
4
Connect to the lesson's theme
How does this hadith reinforce Ghazali's claim that some things are beyond intellect? The burning effect suggests direct perception of divine attributes is overwhelming.

Rational Proof for Metaphorical Interpretation of Divine Attributes
Al-Ghazali uses rational argument to show that 'fingers' and 'kun' must be interpreted metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ghazali uses rational arguments to demonstrate that certain Quranic descriptions of Allah must be interpreted metaphorically to avoid contradiction.

Core Idea
Literal interpretations of divine attributes can lead to impossibility; metaphor preserves transcendence.

Al-Ghazali presents a powerful rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) that certain expressions in the Qur'an and hadith cannot be taken literally. He examines two examples—the mention of 'fingers' in a hadith and the command 'Be' (kun) in the Qur'an—and shows that their literal meanings are impossible. This compels us to interpret them metaphorically (majāz) as referring to Allah's absolute power. In this lesson, we will follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice how al-Ghazali presents two examples.
2
Analyze the argument for 'fingers'
Al-Ghazali says the term 'fingers' is a metaphor for power (qudra). Why does he say this interpretation is more impactful? Think about the impossibility of literal fingers for Allah.
He uses the word 'kinniya' (metonymy).
3
Analyze the argument for 'kun'
Al-Ghazali dissects the verse 'Be, and it is.' He presents two scenarios: if addressed before existence, it's impossible; if after, it's unnecessary. The literal meaning is therefore impossible.
This is a classic rational proof.
4
Understand the conclusion
Al-Ghazali concludes that these expressions are used for their rhetorical impact in conveying Allah's absolute power, not as literal descriptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدرة التي هي سر الأصابع وروحها الخفي، وكنى بالأصابع عن القدرة لأن ذلك أعظم وقعاً في تفهم تمام الإقتدار، ومن هذا القبيل في كنايته عن الإقتدار قوله تعالى: {إِنَّا قَوْلُنَا لِشَيْءٍ إِذَا أَرَدْنَاهُ أَنْ نَقُولَ لَهُ كُنْ فَيَكُونُ} فإن ظاهره ممتنع، إذ قوله: كن إن كان خطاباً للشيء قبل وجوده فهو محال، إذ المعدوم لا يفهم الخطاب حتى يمتثل، وإن كان بعد الوجود فهو مستغن عن التكوين، ولكن لما كانت هذه الكتابة أوقع في النفوس في تفهيم غاية الإقتدار عدل إليها.
Power which is the secret of the fingers and its hidden spirit, and He metaphorically referred to power by 'fingers' because that is more impactful in conveying complete capability. And of this type in His metaphor for capability is His statement, the Most High: 'Indeed, Our word to a thing when We intend it is only that We say to it, "Be," and it is.' For its apparent meaning is impossible, because His saying 'Be' — if it is an address to the thing before its existence, it is impossible, for the non-existent does not understand speech so as to comply; and if it is after existence, it is independent of origination. But since this expression is more striking to souls in conveying utmost capability, He resorted to it.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) that certain Quranic expressions about Allah's attributes must be interpreted metaphorically (majāz) because their literal meaning is impossible. Al-Ghazali examines two examples: the hadith about 'fingers' and the Qur'anic command 'Be' (kun). He argues that attributing fingers to Allah as literal bodily parts is impossible because Allah transcends physical composition. Similarly, the word 'Be' addressed to a thing cannot be taken literally: before the thing exists, it cannot hear the command; after it exists, it does not need a command. Therefore, the only viable interpretation is that these expressions are metaphors (kināyāt) for Allah's complete power and authority. The purpose is to convey the idea of absolute capability in a manner that resonates with human understanding.

Key Terms
مجاز (metaphor)
كناية (metonymy)
القدرة (power)
التكوين (origination)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'fingers' or 'kun' must be interpreted literally because they appear in revelation.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that literal interpretation leads to logical contradiction (impossibility). Therefore, they must be interpreted metaphorically.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Ghazali argue that the literal interpretation of 'kun' (Be) is impossible?
A) Because the verb 'kun' is grammatically incorrect
B) Because it would entail either addressing a non-existent thing or commanding an already existent thing, both absurd
C) Because the verse is abrogated
D) Because Allah never speaks

Key Takeaway
Literal interpretations of certain divine descriptions lead to impossibility, so they must be understood metaphorically to affirm Allah's absolute power and transcendence.

Literal vs. Spiritual Understanding of Creation's Glorification
Ghazali contrasts literal and spiritual understanding of Quranic verses about creation's response to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ghazali's distinction between literal and spiritual interpretations of Quranic verses about creation's glorification, and the hierarchy of understanding among people.

Core Idea
Creation's glorification of God is a language of state, not literal speech.

This passage from Ghazali's Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id addresses a common misunderstanding of Quranic verses that describe creation speaking or glorifying God. Ghazali distinguishes between those who take such expressions literally and those who perceive the spiritual meaning. He then outlines a hierarchy of understanding, showing that even the most learned cannot fully grasp the depth of creation's testimony. This lesson explores that distinction and its implications for theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the two Quranic verses and the different types of people mentioned.
Pay attention to the terms 'balīd' (dull-minded) and 'baṣīr' (insightful).
2
Understand the two verses and two interpretations
Identify the verses (41:11 and 17:44) and how Ghazali contrasts the literal interpretation (assuming life and speech) with the spiritual interpretation (language of state).
The 'language of state' (lisān al-ḥāl) is key.
3
Explore the concept of 'language of state'
Reflect on how Ghazali explains that creation's very existence and need for God is its glorification, using examples like a precise craft testifying to its maker.
Consider how this avoids anthropomorphism while affirming divine transcendence.
4
Examine the three levels of people
Read the part about al-qāṣirūn, al-muqarrabūn, and al-ʿulamāʾ al-rāsikhūn. Note their different degrees of comprehension.
The proximate and rooted scholars understand something but not the full depth.
5
Reflect on the overall lesson
Connect the two parts: how the literal vs spiritual distinction leads to different levels of understanding. Why does Ghazali emphasize this?
This shows the importance of inner insight for proper theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلِلأَرْضِ انْتِيَا طَوْعاً أَوْ كَرْهاً قَالَتَا أَتَيْنَا طَائِعِينَ فالبليد يفتقر في فهمه إلى أن يقدر لهما حياة وعقلاً، وفهما للخطاب وخطاباً هو صوت وحرف تسمعه السماء والارض فتجيبان بحرف وصوت وتقولان: أتينا طائعين والبصير يعلم أن ذلك لسان الحال، وأنه أنباء عن كونهما مسخرتين بالضرورة ومضطرتين الى التسخير ومن هذا قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَإِن مِّن شَيْءٍ إِلَّا يُسَبِّحُ بِحَمْدِهِ ﴾ فالبليد يفتقر فيه إلى أن يقدر للجمادات حياة وعقلا ونطقا بصوت وحرف حتى يقول سبحان الله ليتحقق تسبيحه، والبصير يعلم أنه ما أريد به نطق اللسان، بل كونه مسبحا بوجوده، ومقدسا بذاته، وشاهدا بوحدانية الله سبحانه، كما يقال: وفي كل شيء له آية تدل على أنه الواحد وكما يقال: هذه الصنعة المحكمة تشهد لصانعها بحسن التدبير وكمال العلم بمعنى أنها تقول أشهد بالقول، ولكن بالذات والحال. وكذلك: ما من شيء إلا وهو محتاج في نفسه إلى موجد يوجده ويبقيه ويديم أوصافه ويردده في أطواره فهو بحاجته يشهد لخالقه بالتقديس، يدرك شهادته ذوو البصائر دون الجامدين على الظواهر وذلك قال تعالى: ﴿ ولكن لا تَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ ﴾ وأما القاصرون فلا يفقهون أصلا وأما المقربون والعلماء الراسخون فلا يفقهون كنهه وكماله، إذ لكل شيء شهادات شتى على تقديس الله سبحانه وتسبيحه، ويدرك كل واحد بقدر عقله وبصيرته وتعداد تلك الشهادات لا يليق بعلم المعاملة فهذا الفن أيضاً مما يتفاوت أرباب الظواهر وأرباب البصائر في علمه، وتظهر به مفارقة الباطن للظاهر
And to the earth: 'Come willingly or unwillingly.' They said, 'We come willingly.' So the dull-minded one, in understanding this, needs to assume for them life and intellect, and understanding of speech, and speech that is sound and letter which the heaven and earth hear, so they respond with letter and sound and say: 'We come willingly.' But the insightful one knows that this is the language of state, and that it is an indication of their being subjugated necessarily and compelled to subjugation. And from this is His saying, the Exalted: 'And there is not a thing except that it glorifies [Him] with His praise.' So the dull-minded one, in understanding this, needs to assume for inanimate objects life, intellect, and speech with sound and letter until they say 'Subhan Allah' so that their glorification is realized. But the insightful one knows that what is intended is not the speech of the tongue, but rather its being glorifying by its existence, holy by its essence, and witnessing to the oneness of Allah, the Exalted, as it is said: 'And in everything there is a sign that indicates that He is the One.' And as it is said: 'This precise craftsmanship testifies to its maker of good planning and complete knowledge,' meaning that it says 'I testify' by speech, but by essence and state. Likewise: There is no thing except that it is in itself in need of a bringer-into-existence who brings it into existence, preserves it, perpetuates its attributes, and cycles it through its stages. So by its need it testifies to its Creator with sanctification. People of insight perceive its testimony, not those stuck on outward appearances. And that is because He, the Exalted, said: 'But you do not comprehend their glorification.' As for the deficient, they do not comprehend at all. As for the proximate ones and the firmly rooted scholars, they do not comprehend its ultimate reality and perfection, for everything has manifold testimonies to the sanctification and glorification of Allah, the Exalted, and each one grasps according to his intellect and insight. And enumerating those testimonies is not befitting for the science of transaction. So this field is also one in which the people of outward forms and the people of inner insight differ in their knowledge, and it manifests the distinction of the inner from the outer.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is an extended explanation of how to properly understand Quranic verses that attribute speech and action to created things. Ghazali uses two key verses: the command to the heavens and earth to come (Quran 41:11) and the statement that everything glorifies God (Quran 17:44). He contrasts two types of people: the 'dull-minded' (al-balīd) who take such language literally, assuming that created things have life, intellect, and literal speech with sounds and letters, and the 'insightful' (al-baṣīr) who understand that this is the 'language of state' (lisān al-ḥāl)—the inherent testimony of creation to its Creator through its very existence and need. Ghazali then extends the argument to the broader principle: everything, by its need for a creator, perpetually witnesses to God's sanctity. He then distinguishes three levels of understanding: the 'deficient' (al-qāṣirūn) understand nothing, the 'proximate' (al-muqarrabūn) and 'firmly rooted scholars' (al-ʿulamāʾ al-rāsikhūn) understand something but not the full depth, because each thing has manifold testimonies and each person comprehends according to his capacity. The passage concludes that this is a field where the people of outward forms and people of inner insight differ, revealing the distinction between the outer and inner dimensions of understanding.

Key Terms
لسان الحال
البليد
البصير
أرباب الظواهر
أرباب البصائر
القاصرون
المقربون
العلماء الراسخون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many people think that Quranic verses about creation speaking mean literal speech with sounds and letters, as if inanimate objects possess life and intellect.
Correction: Ghazali explains that such expressions are metaphorical (language of state) and that creation's glorification is through its very existence and need for God, not literal utterance.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what does the 'language of state' (lisān al-ḥāl) refer to?
A) Literal speech of created things
B) The inherent testimony of created things to their Creator
C) The speech of angels
D) The sound of thunder

Key Takeaway
The true glorification of creation is through its very existence and need for God, not literal speech, and people's understanding of this varies according to their insight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses like 'the heavens and earth said, we come willingly' (Quran 41:11), have you ever felt a tension between taking it literally and seeing it as metaphor? How does Ghazali's explanation of 'language of state' challenge you to see the entire universe as a constant, silent witness to God—even when you are heedless? Will you commit to pausing today to reflect on one object around you and see its testimony of need for God?
?
This passage says that every thing glorifies God by its very existence. If your own existence is a testimony, what does it say about God right now? Are you living in a way that your being loudly declares His oneness and your absolute dependence on Him, or have you obscured that testimony with self-sufficiency and distraction? What one change can you make today to align your existence with this testimony?
?
Ghazali notes that even the firmly rooted scholars do not fully comprehend creation's glorification. Does this humbling truth make you restless for deeper knowledge, or does it make you content with surface-level faith? Are you willing to remain a lifelong student of God's signs, knowing you will never fully arrive, or do you secretly wish for complete certainty without effort? How can you embrace this humility today?

All Things Testify to God's Transcendence
Ghazali explains how all things testify to God's transcendence through their need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how all created things, through their inherent neediness, testify to God's transcendence, and why this testimony is only perceived by people of insight.

Core Idea
Everything’s need points to God’s perfection — only insight sees it.

Ghazali invites us to look beyond the surface of things. Every created being is utterly dependent on its Creator for existence and continuity. This very dependence is a silent testimony to God's absolute independence and transcendence. But not everyone can read this testimony; it requires inner insight (basira).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let Ghazali's words sink in.
2
Identify the key claim
What is the central argument? Everything in creation is needy, and that need testifies to God's transcendence.
3
Note the three levels of perception
Ghazali describes three categories: the deficient (understand nothing), the elite (don't grasp the innermost reality), and people of insight (perceive the testimony but not fully).
This shows that even the scholars have limits.
4
Reflect on why this belongs to inner science
Ghazali says enumerating all testimonies is not for the science of practice. Think about why this knowledge is part of unveiling (mukashafa) rather than outward practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو محتاج في نفسه إلى موجد يوجده ويبقيه ويديم أوصافه ويردده في أطواره فهو بحاجته يشهد لخالقه بالتقديس يدرك شهادته ذوو البصائر دون الجامدين على الظواهر وذلك قال تعالى : ولكن لا تَفْقَهُونَ تَسْبِيحَهُمْ وأما القاصرون فلا يفقهون أصلا وأما المقربون والعلماء الراسخون فلا يفقهون كنهه وكماله إذ لكل شيء شهادات شتى على تقديس الله سبحانه وتسبيحه ويدرك كل واحد بقدر عقله وبصيرته وتعداد تلك الشهادات لا يليق بعلم المعاملة فهذا الفن أيضاً مما يتفاوت أرباب الظواهر وأرباب البصائر في علمه وتظهر به مفارقة الباطن للظاهر
And it is in itself in need of an originator who brings it into existence and preserves it and perpetuates its attributes and moves it through its stages. So by its need it testifies to its Creator with transcendence. People of insight perceive its testimony, not those stuck on external appearances. And that is why He said: 'but you do not understand their glorification.' And as for the deficient, they understand nothing at all. And as for those brought near and the deeply rooted scholars, they do not comprehend its innermost reality and perfection. For every thing has various testimonies to the transcendence and glorification of God, and each person perceives according to the measure of his intellect and insight. And the enumeration of those testimonies is not befitting for the science of practice. This art too is one in which the people of externals and the people of insight differ in their knowledge, and through it the distinction between the inner and outer becomes apparent.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of how all created things testify to God's transcendence. Ghazali begins by stating that every thing is inherently needy: it requires an originator to bring it into existence, sustain it, preserve its attributes, and move it through its stages. This very neediness (حاجة) serves as a testimony (شهادة) to its Creator's holiness and transcendence (تقديس). However, only people of insight (ذوو البصائر) perceive this testimony; those who are stuck on external appearances (الجامدون على الظواهر) do not. He supports this with the Quranic verse (17:44): 'but you do not understand their glorification.' Then he distinguishes three levels: the deficient (القاصرون) understand nothing at all; those brought near (المقربون) and the deeply rooted scholars (العلماء الراسخون) do not comprehend the innermost reality (كنه) and perfection (كمال) of this testimony—meaning even the elite cannot fully grasp the complete depth of it. This indicates that the full reality of creation's testimony is known only to God. He further explains that every thing has multiple testimonies, and each person perceives according to his own intellect and insight. Enumerating all these testimonies belongs to the science of unveiling (علم المكاشفة), not the science of practice (علم المعاملة). Thus, this art, like others, differentiates those focused on externals from those with inner insight, and reveals the difference between the apparent and the hidden.

Correction: Ghazali insists the testimony is real and objective, but it requires inner insight (basira) to perceive. Those stuck on external appearances or deficient in understanding do not perceive it. Even the scholars have limits in grasping its full reality.

The Fourth Principle: God is Not a Substance
Ghazali defines the fourth principle and proves God is not a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali argues that God cannot be a substance, based on the nature of substances being subject to motion and rest.

Core Idea
If God were a substance, He would occupy space and change — impossible for the Eternal.

We now turn to the fourth principle of divine transcendence: that God is not a substance. This may seem like a technical point, but it cuts to the heart of how we think about God's relationship to the physical world. Ghazali presents a concise proof that substances are necessarily tied to location and change, and therefore the Eternal cannot be one.

Guided Path
1
Read the original passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the literal translation. Notice how Ghazali states the conclusion first.
2
Analyze the proof structure
Break down the proof: premise 1: every substance occupies space. premise 2: if it occupies space, it is either at rest or in motion. premise 3: both motion and rest are originated (accidents). conclusion: substances are originated, so God cannot be a substance.
This is a classic kalam argument based on the impossibility of pre-eternal accidents.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: why does this matter for theology? It safeguards God's transcendence from all material attributes.
This principle is foundational for later discussions on divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلم بأنه تعالى ليس بجوهر يتحيز، بل يتعالى ويتقدس عن مناسبة الحيز. وبرهانه أن كل جوهر متحيز فهو مختص بحيزه، ولا يخلو من أن يكون ساكنا فيه أو متحركا عنه، فلا يخلو عن الحركة أو السكون وهما حادثان.
Knowledge that He, exalted, is not a substance occupying space, but rather He is exalted and sanctified above association with space. Its proof: every substance occupying space is specific to its space, and it cannot be devoid of being at rest in it or moving from it, so it cannot be free from motion or rest, and both are originated.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth principle: God is not a substance (jawhar). The proof is based on the nature of substances: they occupy space (tahayyuz) and are necessarily characterized by either rest or motion, both of which are accidents (hawadith) and thus originated (hadithan). Therefore, anything that cannot be free from originated accidents is itself originated. Since God is eternal, He cannot be a substance. The conclusion is that God is transcendent beyond spatiality and material composition.

Key Terms
جوهر
حيز
سكون
حركة
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'substance' is a neutral term that can be applied to God without issue.
Correction: In kalam, 'substance' (jawhar) inherently implies spatiality (tahayyuz) and being subject to accidents like motion and rest, which are originated. Applying it to God would imply change and contingency, contradicting His eternity and transcendence.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of Ghazali's proof that God is not a substance?
A) Substances are composed of parts.
B) Every substance is subject to motion or rest, which are originated.
C) Substances are limited in number.
D) Substances cannot exist without accidents.

Key Takeaway
God is not a substance because substances are inherently spatial and subject to change, both incompatible with divinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not a substance and does not occupy space, then He is not 'somewhere' in the universe. Do you ever unconsciously imagine Allah as being 'up above' or 'in a particular direction'? How can you train your heart to worship a God who is not confined to any place?
?
The lesson shows that anything that changes (like rest or motion) is created and cannot be eternal. Think about your own life: what do you cling to that is constantly changing? Are you placing your hope in things that are created and perishable, rather than in the Eternal who never changes?
?
The proof that God is not a substance depends on rigorous reasoning from revelation. Do you take time to understand the intellectual foundations of your faith, or do you rely only on emotions and traditions? What step will you take this week to strengthen your certainty through knowledge?

Fourth Principle: Allah is Not a Substance
Ghazali defines and proves the fourth principle: Allah is not a substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali proves that Allah cannot be a substance, based on the argument that substances entail spatial occupation and temporal change, both of which are incompatible with divine eternity.

Core Idea
Allah cannot be a substance because substances are temporal, but Allah is eternal.

In this fourth principle, Imam al-Ghazali addresses a key term from kalam: substance (jawhar). He affirms that Allah is not a substance, and he provides a logical proof from the nature of substances. This proof is foundational for rejecting any notion that Allah occupies space or is subject to change.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the definition and the proof structure.
The passage contains footnote markers (٤), (٥), (١) that refer to earlier principles.
2
Identify the definition
Notice that Ghazali begins by stating the principle: Allah is not a substance that occupies space. Understand what 'substance' (جوهر) means in this context.
Substance here means something that occupies space and is subject to spatial attributes.
3
Trace the proof steps
Follow the proof: every substance occupies space, so it is either at rest or in motion. Both rest and motion are temporal events. Therefore, any substance is temporal. Since Allah is eternal, He cannot be a substance.
The proof relies on the first principle (that whatever does not avoid temporal events is temporal).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why the proof leads to the impossibility of Allah being a substance. Also note that it implies the impossibility of spatial attributes like direction for Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الرابع : التنزه عن كونه جوهراً العلم بأنه تعالى ليس بجوهر يتحيز (٤) ، بل يتعالى وبتقدس عن مناسبة الحيز وبرهانه أن كل جوهر متحيز فهو مختص بحيزه ، ولا يخلو من أن يكون ساكنا فيه أو متحركا عنه (٥) فلا يخلو عن الحركة أو السكون وهما حادثان ، وما يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث (١) والحكم بحدوثه ثابت بما قدمنا في الأصل الأول من الدليل ، وقد علم من إستحالة كونه تعالى جوهراً إستحالة لوازم الجوهر عليه تعالى من التحيز ولوازمه كالجهة .
The fourth principle: Transcendence from being a substance. Knowledge that He, exalted, is not a substance occupying space (٤), but rather He is exalted and sanctified from appropriateness of space. And its proof is that every substance occupying space is specific to its space, and it does not avoid being either at rest in it or moving from it (٥). Thus it does not avoid motion or rest, both of which are temporal events. And whatever does not avoid temporal events is itself temporal (١). And the judgment of its temporality is established by what we have presented in the first principle from the proof. And it has been known from the impossibility of the Exalted being a substance, the impossibility of the concomitants of substance upon Him, exalted, such as occupying space and its concomitants like direction.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. Ghazali states the fourth principle as affirming that Allah is not a substance that occupies space (جوهر متحيز). He then provides the proof: every substance that occupies space is necessarily specific to its space and cannot avoid either rest or motion within it. Rest and motion are temporal events (حادثان). Anything that cannot avoid temporal events is itself temporal. Therefore, any substance is temporal. Since Allah is eternal, He cannot be a substance. Ghazali references the proof from the first principle for the temporality of that which does not avoid temporal events. The conclusion is that it is impossible for Allah to be a substance, because that would entail occupying space and direction (جهة), which are created attributes.

Key Terms
جوهر
تحيز
حادث
جهة
برهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Substance is a neutral term that can apply to God without contradiction.
Correction: In kalam, substance implies spatial location and temporal change, both of which are incompatible with divine eternity and transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali's proof in the fourth principle, why is it impossible for Allah to be a substance?
A) Because substances are composed of parts.
B) Because substances occupy space and are subject to motion or rest, which are temporal, thus any substance is temporal.
C) Because substances are eternal and thus would challenge divine uniqueness.
D) Because substances are indivisible and thus resemble atoms.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali proves that Allah is not a substance because substances are temporal, and temporality is incompatible with divine eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself imagining Allah as 'somewhere'—above the heavens, in a specific direction—even though you know He is not a substance? How does Ghazali's logical proof challenge that subtle anthropomorphism, and what concrete step can you take today to purify your mental image of Allah from any hint of spatial location?
?
The proof shows that rest and motion are temporal, so Allah cannot be described by them. When you say 'Allah descends' or 'Allah is close,' do you truly mean it in a way that avoids attributing change or movement? How can you train yourself to interpret such scriptural expressions without compromising His transcendence?
?
Ghazali's argument strips away any resemblance between Allah and His creation. Does your worship reflect this absolute uniqueness, or do you secretly feel closer to a God you can 'imagine'? What would it mean for you to worship a God who is utterly unlike anything you can conceive? Are you willing to surrender that comfortable mental picture?

Fifth Principle: Allah is Not a Body
Ghazali defines and proves the fifth principle of divine transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali deduces that Allah is not a body from the denial that He is a substance, and what attributes are necessarily associated with being a body in kalam theology.

Core Idea
A body is composed of substances; Allah is not a substance, so He cannot be a body.

In this passage, Ghazali establishes the fifth principle of Islamic theology: Allah is absolutely transcendent beyond being a body. He builds on the previous principle that Allah is not a substance. By defining a body as a composition of substances, he shows that denying substance logically necessitates denying body. He then explains why substance itself implies limiting attributes like space and composition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the footnote markers: they indicate Ghazali's own cross-references.
2
Understand the definition of 'body'
Ghazali defines a body (جسم) as something composed of substances. Understand that this is a technical kalam definition.
In kalam, a body is not just any physical object but a composite of indivisible parts (atoms).
3
Follow the logical deduction
Since it has been proven that Allah is not a substance, and a body is made of substances, it follows Allah is not a body. Then note the attributes of substance: it entails spatial occupation, divisibility, motion, shape, size—all temporal and impossible for Allah.
This shows how kalam uses rational deduction to affirm divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الخامس : التنزه عن الجسمية العلم بأنه تعالى ليس بجسم مؤلف من جواهر ، إذ الجسم عبارة عن المؤلف من الجواهر، وإذ بطل كونه جوهراً مخصوصاً بحيز بطل كونه جسما (٤)، لأن كل مختص بحيز (٥) ومركب من جوهر ، فالجوهر يستحيل خلوه عن الإفتراق والإجتماع ، والحركة والسكون ، والهيئة والمقدار (٦) .
The fifth principle: Transcendence from being a body. Knowledge that He, exalted, is not a body composed of substances, for a body is an expression of that which is composed of substances. And since it is false that He is a substance specific to a space, it is false that He is a body (٤), because everything specific to a space (٥) is composed of substance. For a substance cannot avoid separation and combination, motion and rest, configuration and magnitude (٦).
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a definition and argument by entailment. Ghazali establishes the fifth principle: Allah is not a body (جسم). He defines a body as a composition of substances. Since it has already been proven in the fourth principle that Allah is not a substance (جوهر) – and specifically not a substance that occupies a space – it follows necessarily that He is not a body, because a body is nothing but a structure made of substances. Moreover, anything that occupies space must be composed of substances. Ghazali then lists the inevitable concomitants of a substance: it cannot be free from separation and combination (i.e., divisibility), motion and rest, configuration (shape) and magnitude (size). All these are temporal attributes incompatible with the divine. Thus, the impossibility of Allah being a body is established.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
تأليف
إفتراق
إجتماع
هيئة
مقدار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God could be a body because scripture uses anthropomorphic language.
Correction: Ghazali argues that body necessarily implies composition and dependence on space, which are impossible for the Creator.

Checkpoint
Why does Ghazali argue that the denial of Allah being a substance leads to the denial of Him being a body?
A) Because a body is a collection of substances, so without substance there can be no body.
B) Because a body is temporal and substances are eternal.
C) Because bodies are divisible and substances are not.
D) Because both are equally impossible for God.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali’s argument that Allah is not a body is logically derived from the denial of substance, reinforcing divine transcendence in rational theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah during prayer, do you ever catch yourself forming a mental image or shape? How does the truth that Allah is not a body challenge that habit, and what practical step will you take today to purify your imagination?
?
The proof shows that a substance cannot be free from separation, motion, and change. Are there any ways you unknowingly attribute limitation or change to Allah in your heart? How can you actively replace such thoughts with the conviction of His absolute transcendence?
?
If you truly internalize that Allah is not composed of parts, how would it change the way you trust Him? Would you still feel that He is 'close' even without physical form? Write one specific thought or action you will adopt to deepen your reliance on the incomparable Creator.

The Fifth Principle: Allah is not a Body
Ghazali defines 'jism' and proves Allah is not a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam al-Ghazali's proof that Allah cannot be a body, based on the definition of body and the impossibility of temporal attributes in the divine.

Core Idea
Allah cannot be a body because bodies are composed of atoms bearing marks of temporality.

This lesson examines the fifth principle of Imam al-Ghazali's Qawa'id al-'Aqa'id. The passage defines the term 'jism' (body) and presents a concise argument that Allah, being eternal, cannot be subject to the temporal attributes inherent in material composition. Understanding this proof is crucial for the Islamic doctrine of divine transcendence (tanzih).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of Ghazali's proof.
2
Identify the definition of jism
Ghazali defines 'body' as that which is composed of atoms (jism 'ibarah 'an al-mu'allaf min al-jawahir). Understand this definition clearly.
This definition is essential for the argument.
3
Follow the logical chain
The argument proceeds: Since Allah cannot be an atom (jawhar) confined to a place, He cannot be composed of atoms, hence cannot be a body. Trace each step.
The impossibility of Allah being a jawhar is already established.
4
Reflect on the temporal attributes
Ghazali lists attributes that atoms cannot be free from: separation/combination, motion/stillness, shape/quantity. These are marks of temporal origination (huduth).
These attributes are incompatible with Allah's eternal nature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الخامس : التنزه عن الجسمية العلم بأنه تعالى ليس بجسم مؤلف من جواهر ، إذ الجسم عبارة عن المؤلف من الجواهر، وإذ بطل كونه جوهراً مخصوصاً بحيز بطل كونه جسما، لأن كل مختص بحيز ومركب من جوهر ، فالجوهر يستحيل خلوه عن الإفتراق والإجتماع ، والحركة والسكون ، والهيئة والمقدار . وهذه سمات الحدوث .
The Fifth Principle: Transcendence from Corporeality. The knowledge that He, exalted, is not a body composed of atoms (jawahir), for 'body' (jism) is an expression for that which is composed of atoms. And since it is invalid that He be an atom (jawhar) confined to a place, it is invalid that He be a body, because everything confined to a place and composed of an atom — the atom cannot be free from separation and combination, motion and stillness, shape and quantity. And these are marks of temporal origination (huduth).
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof that Allah is not a body. Imam al-Ghazali begins the fifth principle (al-asl al-khamis) by stating the knowledge (al-ilm) that Allah is not a body composed of atoms (jawahir). He defines 'body' as that which is composed of atoms. Then he argues: since it has already been proven that Allah cannot be an atom (jawhar) confined to a place (hiz), it follows that He cannot be a body. The reasoning is that anything confined to a place and composed of an atom is subject to certain attributes: separation and combination, motion and stillness, shape and quantity. These attributes are marks of temporal origination (huduth). Since Allah is eternal and necessary, He cannot be subject to temporal marks, so He cannot be a body.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
تحيز
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'body' in Arabic simply implies existence, not necessarily composition.
Correction: Ghazali clarifies that 'jism' means composed of atoms, which entails temporal attributes, so Allah cannot be a body.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Ghazali, why cannot Allah be a body?
A) Because a body is composed of atoms and necessarily has temporal attributes.
B) Because the term 'body' is not found in the Quran.
C) Because bodies are always limited in space.
D) Because the Prophet said so.

Key Takeaway
Allah is not a body because bodies are composed of atoms that necessarily exhibit temporal attributes, which are incompatible with His eternal nature.

?

5 min
The Absurdity of Attributing Corporeality to Allah
Ghazali uses reductio and warns against misusing the term 'body'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming Allah is a body leads to logical absurdity and why using the term 'body' even metaphorically is a mistake.

Core Idea
If Allah were a body, any body could be worshipped. That proves He is not.

Imam al-Ghazali uses a reductio ad absurdum to show the danger of corporealist beliefs. He then addresses a subtle error: those who use the term 'body' without intending composition are still wrong in the name, even if their underlying meaning is correct. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reductio argument
Read the first part: 'If it were permissible to believe that the Maker of the world is a body, then it would be permissible to believe in the divinity of the sun and moon.' Why does this follow?
This is a logical consequence: if Allah is a body, then any body could be divine, which is false.
2
The subtle mistake in naming
Notice that some might call Allah a 'body' without meaning composition from atoms. Ghazali calls this a mistake in the name, even though the intended meaning (not a composite body) is correct.
This shows that accuracy about the name is important, even if the meaning is right.
3
Connect to the broader lesson
Reflect on why Ghazali emphasizes this point: to protect divine transcendence from any hint of anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو جاز أن يعتقد أن صانع العالم جسم لجاز أن يعتقد الالهية للشمس والقمر ، أو لشيء آخر من أقسام الأجسام ، فإن تجاسر متجاسر على تسميته تعالى جسمًا من غير إرادة التأليف من الجواهر ، كان ذلك غلطا في الاسم ، مع الإصابة في نفي معنى الجسم. (٢) وقال لا كالأجسام يعني في لوازم الجسمية كبعض الكرامية والحنابلة حيث قالوا : هو جسم بمعنى موجود ، أو بمعنى أنه قائم بنفسه (٣) وإمتناع إطلاق كل من الجسم والجوهر ظاهر على قول القائلين بالتوقيف وأما على القول بجواز إطلاق المشتق مما ثبت سمعاً إتصافه بمعناه وما يشعر بالجلال ، ولم يوهم نقصاً ، وإن لم يرد توقيف كما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة ، وأبو بكر الباقلاني مخطأ أيضاً ، لأنه لم يوجد في السمع ما يسوغ إطلاقه ، ولأن شرطه بعد السمع أن لا يوهم نقصاً
And if it were permissible to believe that the Maker of the world is a body, then it would be permissible to believe in the divinity of the sun and the moon, or of any other class of bodies. And if someone boldly calls Him, exalted, a body without intending composition from atoms, that is a mistake in the name, while being correct in negating the meaning of 'body.' (2) And he said: 'not like bodies' meaning in the necessary concomitants of corporeality, as some of the Karramiyya and Hanabila said: He is a body in the sense of existing, or in the sense that He is self-subsistent. (3) And the prohibition of applying both 'body' and 'atom' is apparent according to those who adhere to the principle of tawqif (restriction to revealed texts). However, according to the opinion that it is permissible to apply a derived term from what has been established through revelation as having a meaning of glorification that does not imply deficiency, even if no explicit restriction came — as the Mu'tazila and Abu Bakr al-Baqillani held — he is also mistaken, because there is nothing in revelation that permits its application, and because its condition after revelation is that it must not imply deficiency.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is an elaboration on the consequences of attributing corporeality to Allah and a discussion on the permissibility of using the terms 'jism' or 'jawhar' for Him. Imam al-Ghazali begins by showing the absurdity: if it were allowed to believe that the Creator is a body, then logically one could also believe in the divinity of the sun, moon, or any other body. This is a reductio ad absurdum. Then he addresses those who might call Allah a 'body' but without meaning composition from atoms — they are mistaken in the name but correct in negating the actual meaning of 'body'. Then he mentions that some groups (like Karramiyya and Hanabila) say He is a body in the sense of being existent or self-subsistent. Finally, he discusses the prohibition of applying these terms. For those who hold that naming Allah must be based on revelation (tawqif), it is clearly prohibited. For those like the Mu'tazila and al-Baqillani who allow derived terms if they imply glorification and not deficiency, even they are wrong because there is no revealed basis for it, and the condition is that it must not imply deficiency — which it does.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
تأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Ghazali say about believing the Maker of the world is a body?
A) It is permissible
B) It leads to absurdity because then any body could be divine
C) It is permissible if one does not mean composition
D) It is the view of the Hanabila

Key Takeaway
Claiming Allah is a body leads to logical absurdity, and even using the term without meaning composition is a mistake in naming.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used casual or imprecise language about Allah without considering the implications? How does Ghazali's warning about the term 'body' make you re-evaluate the words you use when describing Allah?
?
If someone told you that calling Allah 'a body' in a metaphorical sense is harmless, how would Ghazali's argument change your response? Will you now correct such speech when you hear it, even if the speaker means well?
?
What does your choice of words about Allah reveal about the depth of your understanding of His transcendence? After this lesson, what concrete step will you take to ensure your descriptions of Allah are always precise and respectful?

The Prohibition of Applying 'Body' and 'Atom' to Allah
Ghazali discusses alternative interpretations and the prohibition of terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why terms like 'body' and 'atom' cannot be applied to Allah, even if some groups try to use them with altered meanings.

Core Idea
Revelation does not permit using 'body' for Allah, and it implies deficiency.

Ghazali continues by addressing groups like the Karramiyya and Hanabila who call Allah a 'body' but mean 'existent' or 'self-subsistent'. He then discusses the prohibition of applying such terms, critiquing both the literalists (tawqif) and the rationalists (Mu'tazila and Baqillani) who allow derived terms under certain conditions. This lesson explains why all such attempts fail.

Guided Path
1
Understand the groups' claims
Read the part about Karramiyya and Hanabila who say Allah is a body meaning existent or self-subsistent. Why is this problematic?
They are trying to affirm existence without composition, but the term still carries unwanted connotations.
2
The tawqif position
The next part explains that according to those who require naming Allah to be based on revelation, applying 'body' or 'atom' is clearly prohibited.
This is the strict view: we cannot use terms not found in Quran/Sunnah.
3
The Mu'tazila and Baqillani position
Some, like Mu'tazila and Baqillani, allow derived terms if they imply glorification and no deficiency, even without explicit revelation. Ghazali criticizes them.
He says they are also mistaken because no revelation permits it, and the condition 'no deficiency' is not satisfied.

Source Text

And if it were permissible to believe that the Maker of the world is a body, then it would be permissible to believe in the divinity of the sun and the moon, or of any other class of bodies. 5 min
Allah is Not an Accident
Definition and proof that Allah is not an accident

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'accident' in Islamic theology and why Allah cannot be described as an accident.

Core Idea
An accident needs a body; Allah needs none.

Imam al-Ghazali presents the sixth principle: Allah transcends being an accident. He defines an accident as something that inheres in a body, then proves that the Creator cannot be dependent on creation. This lesson unpacks that argument and clarifies the meaning of 'residing' (hulul).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the overall flow. Note how Ghazali defines 'accident' and then argues against it being applicable to Allah.
Pay attention to the term 'hulul' (residing) and its clarification.
2
Understand the definition of 'accident'
Ghazali defines an accident as 'that which inheres in a body.' Understand that in kalam, accidents are contingent attributes that cannot exist independently.
Accidents are temporally originated (hadith) and dependent on a body.
3
Follow the argument
The argument: Every body is temporal (hadith), so its originator must exist before it. If Allah were an accident, He would need a body, contradicting His pre-eternity and self-sufficiency.
The proof is given from two aspects; the first is contained in the passage.
4
Clarify 'hulul'
Ghazali specifies that 'residing' (hulul) means stability, like an atom residing in a place. This refutes any notion of Allah being spatially located in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل السادس : التنزه عن كونه عرضا العلم بأنه تعالى ليس بعرض قائم بجسم أو حال في محل(٤) ، لأن العرض ما يحل في الجسم ، فكل جسم فهو حادث لا محالة ، ويكون محدثه موجوداً قبله ، فكيف يكون حالاً في الجسم ، وقد كان موجوداً في الأزل وحده وما معه (٤) والمراد بالحلول هنا الإستقرار ومنه حلول الجوهر ، أو الجسم في الحيز ، وإستدل له من وجهين الأول ما تضمنه قوله : لأن العرض يحل في الجسم ، وفي الإقتصاد للمصنف هو ما يحتاج إلى الجسم أو الجوهر في تقومه ، أي في قيام ذواته وتحققها .
The Sixth Principle: Transcendence from Being an Accident. The knowledge that He, exalted, is not an accident (arad) subsisting in a body or residing in a locus, because an accident is that which inheres in a body. So every body is necessarily temporal (hadith), and its originator exists before it. So how could He be residing in a body, when He existed in pre-eternity alone with nothing else? (4) And what is meant by 'residing' (hulul) here is stability, from which comes the residing of an atom or a body in a place. And the proof is derived from two aspects: the first is what is contained in his statement: 'because an accident inheres in a body.' And in al-Iqtisad by the author, it is what needs a body or an atom for its subsistence (taqawwum), i.e., for the establishment of its essence and its actualization.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof that Allah is not an accident (arad). Imam al-Ghazali begins the sixth principle by stating the knowledge that Allah is not an accident subsisting in a body or residing in a locus. He defines an accident as that which inheres in a body. Then he argues: every body is necessarily temporal (hadith), and its originator (muhdith) exists before it. How then can the originator reside in the body, when He existed in pre-eternity alone with nothing else? The passage also clarifies that 'residing' (hulul) means stability, like the residing of an atom in a place. The proof is given in two aspects; the first is that an accident inheres in a body, and the second (not fully given here) is that in al-Iqtisad, an accident is defined as what needs a body for its existence. Since Allah is self-sufficient and pre-eternal, He cannot be an accident.

Key Terms
عرض
حلول
جسم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'accident' here means a random event or chance occurrence.
Correction: In Islamic theology, an 'accident' (arad) is a transient attribute that depends on a body for its existence, not a random event.

Checkpoint
Why can Allah not be an accident according to Ghazali?
A) Because an accident inheres in a body, but Allah existed before all bodies.
B) Because accidents are always temporary.
C) Because accidents are created by Allah, so He cannot be one.
D) All of the above.

Key Takeaway
Allah cannot be an accident because an accident depends on a body, but Allah is pre-eternal and self-sufficient.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah in your mind, do you find yourself unconsciously picturing a form or location? How does this lesson's direct statement—that He existed in pre-eternity alone with nothing else—challenge that habit, and what concrete step will you take to train your heart to think of Him as transcendent?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is not an accident and does not depend on anything, then why do you often fear the loss of created things? What would change in your daily life if you acted on the certainty that Allah alone is self-sufficient and eternal?
?
The proof in this passage begins with defining an accident and then showing its dependence on a body. Are there 'accidents' in your own life—things that depend on others for their existence—that you have mistakenly elevated to the level of ultimate importance? What will you do to redirect that reliance solely to the Creator?
