The Rational Argument Against Corporeality
Argument from marks of origination against Allah being a body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the marks of origination (ḥudūth) make it impossible for Allah to be a body, and why such a belief leads to absurdity.

Core Idea
The marks of origination prove Allah is not a body.

This passage from Imam al-Ghazali presents a concise rational argument against the corporeality of Allah. By noting that bodies share originatedness, he shows that attributing bodyhood to the Creator would make Him comparable to created things like the sun and moon—a clear violation of divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument from marks of origination
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how Al-Ghazali begins by stating that the marks of origination (e.g., change, limitation) are incompatible with divinity.
2
Analyze the analogy with the sun and moon
Consider the logical implication: if Allah were a body, then any body could be a god. This reductio ad absurdum shows the error.
3
Connect to the broader lesson
Reflect on how this argument fits into Islamic theology's emphasis on tanzīh (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه سمات الحدوث (۱) ، ولو جاز أن يعتقد أن صانع العالم جسم لجاز أن يعتقد الالهية للشمس والقمر ، أو لشيء آخر من أقسام الأجسام ، فإن تجاسر متجاسر على تسميته تعالى جسمًا من غير إرادة التأليف من الجواهر (۲) ، كان ذلك غلطا في الاسم ، مع الإصابة في نفي معنى الجسم(٣) وإمتناع إطلاق كل من الجسم والجوهر ظاهر على قول القائلين بالتوقيف وأما على القول بجواز إطلاق المشتق مما ثبت سمعاً إتصافه بمعناه وما يشعر بالجلال ، ولم يوهم نقصاً ، وإن لم يرد توقيف كما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة ، وأبو بكر الباقلاني مخطأ أيضاً ، لأنه لم يوجد في السمع ما يسوغ إطلاقه ، ولأن شرطه بعد السمع أن لا يوهم نقصاً فيكتغون حيث لا بدلالة العقل على إتصافه تعالى بمعنى ذلك اللفظ سمع
And these are marks of origination (1). If it were permissible to believe that the Maker of the world is a body, it would be permissible to believe in the divinity of the sun and the moon, or of anything else among the divisions of bodies. So if someone ventures to call Him, may He be exalted, a body without intending composition from atoms (2), that would be a mistake in name, while being correct in negating the meaning of body (3). And the impossibility of applying both 'body' and 'substance' is clear according to the position of those who require a scriptural warrant. But as for the position of permissibility of applying a derived term based on something established by revelation to be an attribute of Him with its meaning and which suggests majesty and does not imply deficiency, even if no scriptural warrant has come—as the Mu'tazila held—Abu Bakr al-Baqillani also erred, because there is nothing in revelation that permits its usage, and because its condition after revelation is that it should not imply deficiency, so they fall short when there is no rational indication that He, exalted, is described by the meaning of that term.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that Allah is a body and addresses objections. It begins by stating that marks of origination (ḥudūth) are incompatible with necessary existence. Then it argues that if it were permissible to believe the Creator is a body, then one could just as well claim divinity for the sun or moon, since all bodies are alike in being originated. For those who insist on calling Him a body but do not mean He is composed of atoms (like some Karramiyya and Hanabila who say He is a body meaning 'existing' or 'self-subsistent'), the author says this is a mistake in terminology, although they are correct in negating the true meaning of body (composition). The discussion then turns to the permissibility of applying the terms 'body' or 'substance' to Allah. According to those who require scriptural warrant (tawqīf), it is forbidden because no such term has been revealed. According to those who allow deriving terms from revealed attributes as long as they do not imply deficiency (like the Mu'tazila and al-Baqillani), even they are wrong because there is no revelatory permission, and the condition that it not imply deficiency is not met since the term can lead to misunderstanding. The conclusion is that such terms must not be used.

Key Terms
جسم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what follows from believing the Maker of the world is a body?
A) It would be permissible to believe in the divinity of the sun and moon.
B) It would be permissible to believe that He is eternal.
C) It would be permissible to deny His attributes.
D) It would be permissible to call Him a substance.

Key Takeaway
The marks of origination demonstrate that Allah cannot be a body, for that would make Him comparable to contingent things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine Allah in your mind, do you ever unconsciously picture Him as having a form or location? How does this argument dismantle that mental image and what will you do to correct it?
?
This argument uses pure reason to defend tawheed. Do you trust your intellect enough to let it guide you to the truth about Allah, or do you rely solely on inherited beliefs? What specific step will you take today to deepen your rational understanding of tawheed?
?

The Terminological Debate: Name vs. Meaning
Discussion of naming Allah 'body' and the rules for applying terms to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between calling Allah a body as a name versus as a meaning, and why even metaphorical usage is prohibited by revelation and reason.

Core Idea
Calling Allah a body is wrong even as a name, not just as a meaning.

Al-Ghazali now addresses a nuanced position: some who say Allah is a body do not mean He is composed of atoms, but they use the term loosely. He explains that this is a mistake in terminology, even though they correctly negate the true meaning. The passage then examines the scriptural and rational rules governing names for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction
Read the passage and note the difference between 'mistake in name' and 'correct in negating the meaning of body'. Al-Ghazali acknowledges the intention but rejects the wording.
2
Explore the positions on tawqīf
Learn about the two views: those who require scriptural warrant (tawqīf) and those who allow derived terms (Muʿtazila, al-Bāqillānī).
3
Analyze the critique of al-Bāqillānī
See why even the permissive view is rejected: no scriptural warrant exists, and the term can imply deficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذه سمات الحدوث (۱) ، ولو جاز أن يعتقد أن صانع العالم جسم لجاز أن يعتقد الالهية للشمس والقمر ، أو لشيء آخر من أقسام الأجسام ، فإن تجاسر متجاسر على تسميته تعالى جسمًا من غير إرادة التأليف من الجواهر (۲) ، كان ذلك غلطا في الاسم ، مع الإصابة في نفي معنى الجسم(٣) وإمتناع إطلاق كل من الجسم والجوهر ظاهر على قول القائلين بالتوقيف وأما على القول بجواز إطلاق المشتق مما ثبت سمعاً إتصافه بمعناه وما يشعر بالجلال ، ولم يوهم نقصاً ، وإن لم يرد توقيف كما ذهبت إليه المعتزلة ، وأبو بكر الباقلاني مخطأ أيضاً ، لأنه لم يوجد في السمع ما يسوغ إطلاقه ، ولأن شرطه بعد السمع أن لا يوهم نقصاً فيكتغون حيث لا بدلالة العقل على إتصافه تعالى بمعنى ذلك اللفظ سمع
And these are marks of origination (1). If it were permissible to believe that the Maker of the world is a body, it would be permissible to believe in the divinity of the sun and the moon, or of anything else among the divisions of bodies. So if someone ventures to call Him, may He be exalted, a body without intending composition from atoms (2), that would be a mistake in name, while being correct in negating the meaning of body (3). And the impossibility of applying both 'body' and 'substance' is clear according to the position of those who require a scriptural warrant. But as for the position of permissibility of applying a derived term based on something established by revelation to be an attribute of Him with its meaning and which suggests majesty and does not imply deficiency, even if no scriptural warrant has come—as the Mu'tazila held—Abu Bakr al-Baqillani also erred, because there is nothing in revelation that permits its usage, and because its condition after revelation is that it should not imply deficiency, so they fall short when there is no rational indication that He, exalted, is described by the meaning of that term.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that Allah is a body and addresses objections. It begins by stating that marks of origination (ḥudūth) are incompatible with necessary existence. Then it argues that if it were permissible to believe the Creator is a body, then one could just as well claim divinity for the sun or moon, since all bodies are alike in being originated. For those who insist on calling Him a body but do not mean He is composed of atoms (like some Karramiyya and Hanabila who say He is a body meaning 'existing' or 'self-subsistent'), the author says this is a mistake in terminology, although they are correct in negating the true meaning of body (composition). The discussion then turns to the permissibility of applying the terms 'body' or 'substance' to Allah. According to those who require scriptural warrant (tawqīf), it is forbidden because no such term has been revealed. According to those who allow deriving terms from revealed attributes as long as they do not imply deficiency (like the Mu'tazila and al-Baqillani), even they are wrong because there is no revelatory permission, and the condition that it not imply deficiency is not met since the term can lead to misunderstanding. The conclusion is that such terms must not be used.

Key Terms
جسم
جوهر
توقيف
مشتق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if they do not mean a physical body, it is acceptable to call Allah a body.
Correction: Al-Ghazali shows that even if the meaning is intended correctly, the name itself is a mistake (غلط في الاسم) and is not permitted by revelation or reason.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Abu Bakr al-Baqillani's position wrong?
A) He allows calling Allah a body as long as it implies majesty.
B) He requires a scriptural warrant but there is none.
C) He denies that the term can imply deficiency.
D) He relies on rational proof alone.

Key Takeaway
Even a metaphorical use of 'body' for Allah is a mistake in name and lacks scriptural or rational justification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever carelessly described Allah with a word that does not come from revelation? What does this lesson reveal about the seriousness of that slip, and what will you do to guard your tongue from now on?
?
Ghazali shows that even a 'minor' error in name is a mistake. Does your heart tremble at the thought of misrepresenting Allah? Commit to learning the correct names and attributes of Allah from the Quran and Sunnah today.
?
If you realized you have spoken about Allah in a way that implies deficiency, even unintentionally, what is the first step you must take to repent and correct that pattern?

The Proof that Allah is Not an Accident
Proof that Allah is not an accident using definition and pre-eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that Allah cannot be an accident based on the definition of accident and Allah's pre-eternal existence.

Core Idea
Allah's pre-eternal independence disproves any notion of Him being an accident.

Imam al-Ghazali begins the sixth principle by establishing that Allah is not an accident. He provides a concise proof: an accident inheres in a body, but bodies are originated and require an originator; Allah existed alone before all creation. This short passage lays the foundation for tawhid by negating any similarity between Allah and contingent things.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of accident
Understand what an accident (ʿaraḍ) is: something that subsists in a body. This is the key term for the proof.
Accidents are dependent on bodies for existence.
2
Follow the logical argument
The argument: every body is originated (ḥādith) → it has an originator existing before it → Allah is pre-eternal and alone → He cannot be an accident inhering in a body.
The conclusion follows from the impossibility of the Creator being contained in His creation.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical question
Notice the rhetorical question: 'How could He be a state in a body?' This drives home the absurdity of the opposite view.
4
Connect to broader tawhid
This proof reinforces that Allah is independent and not contingent upon anything, which is essential for Islamic monotheism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل السادس : التنزه عن كونه عرضا العلم بأنه تعالى ليس بعرض قائم بجسم أو حال في محل(٤) ، لأن العرض ما يحل في الجسم ، فكل جسم فهو حادث لا محالة ، ويكون محدثه موجوداً قبله ، فكيف يكون حالاً في الجسم ، وقد كان موجوداً في الأزل وحده وما معه غيره ، ثم أحدث الأجسام والأعراض بعده (۱) ؟ ولأنه عالم قادر مريد خالق (۲) ، وهذه الأوصاف تستحيل على الأعراض بل لا تعقل الا لموجود (۳) قائم بنفسه ، مستقل بذاته ، وقد تحصل من هذه الأصول (٥) أنه موجود (٤) قائم بنفسه ، ليس بجوهر ولا جسم ولا عرض(٥) ، وأن العالم كله جواهر وأعراض وأجسام (٦) فإذا لا يشبه شيئا ولا يشبهه شيء(٧) بل الحي القيوم (٨) (۸) الذي ليس كمثله شيء(۹)
The sixth principle: Transcendence from being an accident. Knowledge that He, exalted, is not an accident subsisting in a body or a state in a locus (4), because the accident is that which inheres in the body. So every body is necessarily originated, and its originator exists before it. So how could He be a state in a body, while He existed in pre-eternity alone with nothing else with Him, and then originated bodies and accidents afterward (1)? And because He is knowing, powerful, willing, creating (2), and these attributes are impossible for accidents; rather, they are only conceivable for an existent (3) self-subsistent, independent in essence. And it has been derived from these principles (5) that He is an existent (4) self-subsistent, not a substance, not a body, and not an accident (5), and that the entire world is substances, accidents, and bodies (6). Thus, nothing resembles Him, and nothing resembles anything (7); rather, He is the Living, the Self-Subsisting (8) who is not like anything (9).
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth principle: Allah's transcendence from being an accident (ʿaraḍ). It first states that we know Allah is not an accident that subsists in a body or inheres in a locus. The proof: an accident is something that inheres in a body, so every body is originated (ḥādith) and must have an originator existing before it. Allah existed eternally alone before creation; how then could He be a state within a body? That would imply dependence and temporality. Second, Allah is described as knowing, powerful, willing, creating—attributes that cannot belong to accidents, which are contingent and perishable. Such attributes require an independent, self-subsistent existent. The passage concludes by summarizing the results from all the principles: Allah is existent, self-subsistent, not a substance (jawhar), not a body (jism), not an accident (ʿaraḍ). The world consists of substances, accidents, and bodies—all created and unlike Him. Thus, nothing resembles Allah, and He is the Living, the Self-Subsisting, of whom there is nothing like.

Key Terms
عرض
حلول
جسم
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Belief that Allah could be an accident inhering in some substance.
Correction: Accidents are contingent and depend on bodies; Allah is pre-eternal and independent, so He cannot be an accident.

Checkpoint
Why can Allah not be an accident according to the passage?
A) Because accidents are always created after bodies.
B) Because accidents inhere in bodies and are originated, while Allah is pre-eternal and independent.
C) Because accidents are invisible.
D) Because accidents cannot be described as living.

Key Takeaway
Allah's pre-eternal existence and independence make it impossible for Him to be an accident.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof shows that Allah existed alone before anything else. Do you truly live as if He alone is sufficient, or do you act as though other things are indispensable to your well-being?
?
This lesson forces you to see that accidents (like your health, wealth, or status) are temporary and dependent. Are you more attached to these passing attributes than to the Eternal One who creates them?

Divine Attributes and Transcendence
Enumeration of divine attributes and conclusion of Allah's transcendence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah's attributes like knowledge and power cannot belong to accidents, and the conclusion that Allah is not like any created thing.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes prove He is not an accident but a self-subsistent, unique being.

After proving Allah is not an accident, Imam al-Ghazali now presents the attributes that are impossible for accidents. He argues that qualities like knowledge, power, and will require an independent, self-subsistent existent. This leads to the final summary: Allah is not a substance, body, or accident, and nothing resembles Him.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divine attributes listed
Note the attributes: knowing (ʿālim), powerful (qādir), willing (murīd), creating (khāliq). These are mentioned to contrast with accidents.
Accidents cannot possess such stable, perfect qualities.
2
Understand why accidents lack these attributes
Accidents are temporary and dependent; they cannot be described with attributes that imply self-sufficiency and permanence.
3
Analyze the concluding summary
From all principles, we derive that Allah is existent, self-subsistent (qā'im bi-nafsih), not a substance, body, or accident. The world is composed of these categories, so Allah is unlike anything.
This affirms tanzih (transcendence).
4
Reflect on the final verse
The passage ends with the Qur'anic phrase 'laysa ka-mithlihi shay'' (nothing is like Him). This is the core of Islamic tawhid.

Seventh Principle: God is Free from Direction
Ghazali states the seventh principle and explains its basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God is free from direction because directions are created and relative to human anatomy, not absolute realities that can confine the Creator.

Core Idea
Directions are created, so God, their Creator, is beyond them.

Ghazali continues his principles of divine transcendence by addressing a common misconception: that God occupies a specific direction. He argues that directions like above, below, right, left, front, and back are not eternal but were created by God through the creation of human beings. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for our understanding of God's transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the English translation. Let Ghazali's own wording set the stage.
Notice the list of six directions.
2
Identify the six directions
The passage names six directions: above, below, right, left, front, and back. Understand that these are the conventional spatial directions.
These are the directions we experience in everyday life.
3
Follow Ghazali's argument
Ghazali says these directions were created by God 'by means of the creation of man.' He gives the example of the human body: feet and head give rise to the concepts of 'above' and 'below'.
Think: why does he use human anatomy?
4
Reflect on the implication
Since directions are created and relative, they cannot apply to God, who is the Creator of all things. God is free from being confined to any direction.
This is the key takeaway for tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل السابع - العلم بأن الله تعالى منزه الذات عن الإختصاص بالجهات، فإن الجهة إما فوق، وإما أسفل، وإما يمين، وإما شمال، وإما قدام، أو خلف، وهذه الجهات هو الذي خلقها وأحدثها بواسطة خلق الإنسان، إذ خلق له طرفين: أحدهما يعتمد على الأرض ويسمى رجلاً، والآخر يقابله رأساً: ويسمى، فحدث إسم الفوق لما يلي جهة الرأس، وإسم السفل لما يلي
The seventh principle: Knowledge that God, exalted is He, is free in essence from being confined to directions. For direction is either above, or below, or right, or left, or front, or back. And these directions He created and brought about by means of the creation of man, as He created for him two ends: one depends on the ground and is called a foot, and the other opposite it is a head, and is called; thus the name 'above' came into being for what is adjacent to the direction of the head, and the name 'below' for what is adjacent to
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the seventh foundational principle: God's essence is free from being confined to any direction (jiha). The author explains that a direction is either above, below, right, left, front, or back. These directions are not eternal; rather, God created them after creating human beings. Specifically, by creating humans with feet (which depend on the ground) and a head (which is opposite), the concepts of 'above' (what is adjacent to the head) and 'below' (what is adjacent to the feet) came into existence. The implication is that directions are relative and created, so God, who is the Creator of directions, cannot be encompassed by them. The passage ends mid-sentence at the page cutoff, continuing the explanation of 'below'.

The Seventh Principle: Allah is Beyond All Directions
Ghazali states the seventh principle and proves it through the creation of human anatomy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why directions are created and relative, and why Allah cannot be described by any direction.

Core Idea
Directions are relative; Allah transcends them all.

Imam al-Ghazali now tackles a critical point: the belief that Allah is free from being confined to any direction. Many assume direction is absolute, but Ghazali shows it is entirely dependent on our human form. This passage will reveal how the very concepts of above, below, right, and left are created and relative, leading to the conclusion that Allah—who is eternal—cannot be described by something created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation once. Notice how Ghazali builds his argument step by step.
2
Identify the six directions
Ghazali lists above, below, right, left, front, and back. Understand how each is defined relative to human anatomy.
He uses the head, feet, hands, and sides to explain each direction.
3
Understand the relativity of directions
Consider the example of the ant crawling upside down: its 'above' becomes our 'below'. This shows direction is not absolute.
This example is key to seeing that direction depends on orientation.
4
Reflect on the spherical thought experiment
If humans were spherical, directions would not exist at all. This proves directions are contingent on human form.
5
Apply the logic to Allah
Since directions are created and relative, the eternal Allah cannot be described by them. He is beyond all spatial attributes.
This is the main conclusion of the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل السابع - العلم بأن الله تعالى منزه الذات عن الإختصاص بالجهات فإن الجهة إما فوق، وإما أسفل، وإما يمين، وإما شمال : أو قدام، أو خلف وهذه الجهات هو الذي خلقها وأحدثها بواسطة خلق الإنسان إذ خلق له طرفين : أحدهما يعتمد على الأرض ويسمى رجلاً ، والآخر يقابله رأساً : ويسمى ، فحدث إسم الفوق لما يلي جهة الرأس وإسم السفل لما يلي جهة الرجل حتى ان النملة التي تدب منكسة تحت السقف تنقلب جهة الفوق في حقها تحتا وإن كان في حقنا فوقا ، وخلق للإنسان اليدين وأحداهما أقوى من الأخرى، فحدث إسم اليمين للأقوى، وإسم الشمال لما الأخرى في الغالب يقابله، وتسمى الجهة التي تلي اليمين يميناً، والاخرى شمالاً، وخلق له جانبين يبصر من أحدهما ويتحرك إليه، فحدث إسم القدام للجهة التي يتقدم إليها بالحركة، وإسم الخلف لما يقابلها : فالجهات حادثة بحدوث الإنسان، ولو لم يخلق الإنسان بهذه الخلقة، بل خلق مستديراً كالكرة، لم يكن لهذه الجهات وجود ألبتة، فكيف كان في الازل مختصا بجهة والجهة حادثة ؟ أو كيف صار مختصاً بجهة بعد أن لم يكن له : أبان خلق العالم فوقه ، وتعالى عن أن يكون له فوق ، إذ تعالى أن يكون له رأس ، والفوق عبارة عما يكون جهة الرأس ، أو خلق العالم تحته ، فتعالى عن أن يكون له تحت ، إذ تعالى عن ان يكون له رجل والتحت عبارة عما يلي الرجل ، وكل ذلك مما يستحيل في العقل
The seventh principle: The knowledge that Allah, exalted is He, is transcendent in essence from being specified by directions. For direction is either above, or below, or right, or left, or front, or behind. And these directions are what He created and originated by means of the creation of man. For He created for him two sides: one of them rests on the ground and is called a foot, and the other opposite it is a head; so the name 'above' came about for what faces the direction of the head, and the name 'below' for what faces the direction of the foot, to the point that the ant that creeps upside down under the ceiling turns the direction of 'above' for itself into 'below', even though for us it is 'above'. And He created for man two hands, one of them stronger than the other, so the name 'right' came about for the stronger, and the name 'left' for the other that generally corresponds to it, and the direction that follows the right is called right, and the other left. And He created for him two sides, from one of which he sees and towards which he moves, so the name 'front' came about for the direction towards which he advances by movement, and the name 'back' for what corresponds to it. So directions are originated by the origination of man. And if man were not created in this form, but were created spherical like a sphere, these directions would have no existence at all. So how could He have been in pre-eternity specified with a direction while direction is originated? Or how did He become specified with a direction after He had none? Would it be because He created the world above Him? Exalted is He above having an above, for He is exalted above having a head, and 'above' is an expression for what is the direction of the head. Or because He created the world below Him? Exalted is He above having a below, for He is exalted above having a foot, and 'below' is an expression for what faces the foot. And all of this is rationally impossible.
Source pages p. 162

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh principle of Aqidah according to Imam al-Ghazali: the knowledge that Allah is transcendent and free from being confined to any direction (jiha). Ghazali begins by listing the six directions: above, below, right, left, front, and back. He then argues that these directions are not eternal or absolute; rather, they are created (haditha) and arise from the specific anatomy of human beings. He explains that the concept of 'above' comes from the relative position of the head, and 'below' from the feet. He gives the example of an ant crawling on the ceiling: for the ant, 'above' becomes its own 'below' relative to its orientation, showing the relativity of direction. Similarly, right and left are based on the stronger hand, and front and back on the direction of movement and sight. Ghazali then makes a powerful logical argument: if man were created spherical, these directions would not exist at all. Therefore, directions are contingent upon human creation. It is impossible for the eternal Allah, who existed before all creation, to be described by a direction, because that would imply that He is characterized by something that is created and relative. The conclusion is clear: attributing direction to Allah is rationally impossible (mustahil fi al-'aql).

Key Terms
جهة (jiha)
فوق (fawq)
تحت (taht)
يمين (yamin)
شمال (shimal)
قدام (qudam)
خلف (khalaf)
حادث (hadith)
أزل (azal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'above' is an absolute direction for Allah, as in some literal interpretations of 'above the Throne'.
Correction: Ghazali shows that 'above' is a relative concept tied to human anatomy. Allah is beyond all directions, including above.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, why do directions like 'above' and 'below' exist?
A) They are eternal and absolute properties of space.

D) They are necessary for describing Allah’s essence.

Key Takeaway
Because directions are created and relative, it is rationally impossible for the eternal Allah to be described by any direction.

The Contingency of Directions Refutes Divine Direction
Ghazali proves directions are relative, created, and thus impossible for the Eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah cannot be in any direction, because directions are relative and created, while Allah is eternal.

Core Idea
Directions are created; the Eternal cannot be contained by the created.

Ghazali uses a striking thought experiment: even an ant crawling upside down under a ceiling has its own 'up' and 'down', proving that directions depend on perspective and anatomy. He then extends this to show that if humans were spherical, directions would not exist at all. From this, he argues that since Allah existed before any creation, He cannot be in a direction. This lesson unfolds this powerful proof for Allah's transcendence beyond space.

Guided Path
1
Observe Ghazali's examples of relative directions
Read the examples of the ant and the human hands. Notice how 'up' changes for the ant, and 'right/left' depend on which hand is stronger.
These examples show that directions are not absolute.
2
Understand the createdness of directions
Ghazali argues that if humans were spherical, directions like up/down, right/left would not exist. This proves directions are created with human anatomy.
If directions were eternal, they would exist even without creatures.
3
Apply the principle to Allah
Since Allah exists in eternity (azal) before any creation, He could not have been in a direction that did not exist. Moreover, being 'above' would imply a head, and 'beneath' would imply a foot—both impossible for Allah.
This refutes anthropomorphic concepts of Allah being in a literal direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جهة الرجل حتى ان النملة التي تدب منكسة تحت السقف تنقلب جهة الفوق في حقها تحتا وإن كان في حقنا فوقا (۱) ، وخلق للإنسان اليدين وأحداهما أقوى من الأخرى ، فحدث إسم اليمين للأقوى (٢) ، وإسم الشمال لما الأخرى في الغالب يقابله (۳) ، وتسمى الجهة التي تلي اليمين يميناً ، والاخرى شمالاً ، وخلق له جانبين يبصر من أحدهما ويتحرك إليه ، فحدث إسم القدام للجهة التي يتقدم إليها بالحركة ، وإسم الخلف لما يقابلها : فالجهات حادثة بحدوث الإنسان(٤) ، ولو لم يخلق الإنسان بهذه الخلقة ، بل خلق مستديراً كالكرة ، لم يكن لهذه الجهات وجود ألبتة (٥) فكيف كان في الازل مختصا بجهة والجهة حادثة (٦) ؟ أو كيف صار مختصاً بجهة بعد أن لم يكن له : أبان خلق العالم فوقه ، وتعالى عن أن يكون له فوق ، إذ تعالى أن يكون له رأس ، والفوق عبارة عما يكون جهة الرأس ، أو خلق العالم تحته ، فتعالى عن أن يكون له تحت ، إذ تعالى عن ان يكون له رجل والتحت عبارة عما يلي الرجل ، وكل ذلك مما يستحيل في العقل (٧)

(۱) ومعنى الفوق فيما يمشي على أربع أو على بطنه بالنسبة إليهما ما يخاذي ظهره من فوقه فهي كلها إضافية .
(۲) أي اليمين ما يحاذي أقوى يديه غالباً
(۳) وإنما قيده بالغالب فإن في الناس من يساره أقوى من اليمين ولكنه نادر
(٤) فقبل خلق العالم لم يكن فوق ولا تحت إذ لم يكن ثم حيوان فلم يكن ثم رأس ولا رجل ولا ظهر وهي مع ذلك إعتبار به لا حقيقة لا تتبدل .
(٥) يعني لم توجد واحدة من هذه إذ لا رأس ولا رجل ولا يمين ولا شمال ولا ظهر ولا وجه
(٦) وهو تعالى كان موجوداً في الأزل ولم يكن شيء من الموجودات لأن كل موجود سواه حادث
(۷) فهذا طريق الإستدلال، قال أبو منصور التميمي : وأما حالة كونه في جهة فإن ذلك كإحالة كونه في مكان لأن ذلك يوجب حدوث كونه ومحاذاة مخصوصة فيه وذلك دليل على حدوث ما حل فيه ، فلذلك أحلنا إطلاق إسم الجهة على الله تعالى .
The direction of the foot, even the ant that crawls upside down under the ceiling, its direction of above becomes below in its respect, even if in our respect it is above (1). And He created for man two hands, one of them stronger than the other, so the name 'yamin' arose for the stronger (2), and the name 'shimal' for the other in the majority of cases (3). And the side that follows the right is called 'yamin', and the other 'shimal'. And He created for him two sides, from one of which he sees and towards which he moves. So the name 'qudam' arose for the direction towards which he advances in movement, and the name 'khalf' for what opposes it. Thus directions are created with the creation of man (4). And if man had not been created with this constitution, but had been created round like a sphere, these directions would not have existed at all (5). So how could He have been in eternity specific to a direction while direction is created (6)? Or how did He become specific to a direction after He had no direction? He created the world above Him, but He is exalted above having an 'above', for He is exalted above having a head, and 'above' is an expression for what is the direction of the head. Or He created the world beneath Him, but He is exalted above having a 'beneath', for He is exalted above having a foot, and 'beneath' is an expression for what is adjacent to the foot. And all of this is impossible in the intellect (7). (Footnote 1: The meaning of 'above' regarding what walks on four or on its belly, relative to them, is what faces its back from above; all of these are relative. (2) i.e., the right is what faces his stronger hand usually. (3) He restricted it to 'usually' because among people there are those whose left is stronger than right, but it is rare. (4) So before the creation of the world there was no above and no beneath, since there was no animal, so there was no head, no foot, no back; and these are [purely] conceptual, not an immutable reality. (5) i.e., none of these would exist, since there is no head, no foot, no right, no left, no back, no front. (6) And He, exalted, existed in eternity and there was nothing of the existents, because every existent other than Him is created. (7) This is the method of proof. Abu Mansur al-Tamimi said: As for the state of His being in a direction, that is like the impossibility of His being in a place, because that would necessitate a particular createdness and a particular adjacency in Him, and that is evidence of the createdness of that in which He inheres; therefore we deem the application of the term 'direction' to Allah, exalted, impossible.)
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contingency. It begins by observing that directions like up/down, right/left, front/back are relative to human anatomy: the ant's 'above' is our 'below', and the right/left distinction depends on which hand is stronger. These directions did not exist before human creation—if humans were spherical, such concepts would not arise. Therefore directions are contingent and created. Since Allah is eternal and existed before any creation, He could not have been in a direction that did not exist. Moreover, if one claims Allah is 'above' the world, that would imply He has a head; if 'beneath', a foot—both impossible. The footnotes reinforce: directions are relative and conceptual, not absolute realities. Abu Mansur al-Tamimi concludes that being in a direction would entail a particular createdness and adjacency, which is impossible for the Eternal.

Correction: Ghazali shows that 'above' is relative to a creature's anatomy; since Allah has no body, He cannot be in a literal direction. Directions are created, so the Eternal cannot be contained by them.

Why Allah Cannot Be in a Direction
Ghazali argues that being in a direction means being either a substance or an accident, both impossible for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of being in a direction is impossible for Allah, as argued by Ghazali.

Core Idea
Being in a direction forces Allah to be a substance or accident—both impossible.

Ghazali presents a crisp logical argument that the concept of 'direction' when applied to Allah leads to an impossibility. He analyses what it means to be in a direction and shows that it can only be understood in two ways, both of which are inapplicable to the Creator. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and the supporting arguments from later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the main argument
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Ghazali defines 'being in a direction' as having only two possible meanings.
The footnotes are part of the original text and reinforce the argument.
2
Understand the two meanings
The two meanings are: occupying space like a substance, or inhering in a substance like an accident. Both are impossible for Allah, as previously established.
3
Reflect on the footnotes
The footnotes by al-Nasafi and al-Subki provide additional reasoning: directions are mutually contradictory, and being in a direction implies being in a place, which implies need—impossible for the Necessary Being.
This reinforces the conclusion from different angles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كونه مختصاً بجهة أن مختص بحيز اختصاص الجواهر ، أو مختص بالجواهر اختصاص العرض ، وقد ظهر إستحالة كونه جوهراً أو عرضاً (۱) ، فاستحال كونه مختصاً بالجهة ، وإن أريد بالجهة (۲) غير هذين المعنيين ، كان غلطاً في الإسم مع على المعنى

(۱) أو جسما إذ الحيز مختص بالجوهر والجسم وقد مر تنزيهه سبحانه عنهما، وأما العرض فلا إختصاص له بالحيز إلا بواسطة كونه حالاً في الجوهر، فهو تابع لإختصاص الجوهر، ولما ظهر بطلان الجوهرية والجسمية.
(۲) وقال النسفي في شرح العمدة : الصور والجهات مختلفة وإجتماعها عليه تعالى مستحيل لتنافيها في أنفسها وليس البعض أولى من البعض لإستواء الكل في إفادة المدح والنقص وعدم دلالة المحدثات عليه ، فلو إختص بشيء منها لكان تخصيص مخصص ، وهذا من أمارات المحدث » اهـ وقال السبكي : صانع العالم لا يكون في جهة لأنه لو كان في جهة لكان في مكان ضرورة إنها المكان أو المستلزمة له ولو كان في مكان لكان متحيزاً ، ولو كان متحيزاً لكان مفتقر إلى حيزه ومكانه فلا يكون واجب الوجود ، وثبت أنه واجب الوجود وهذا خلف وأيضاً فلو كان في جهة فإما في كل الجهات وهو محال وشنيع ، وإما في البعض فيلزم الإختصاص المستلزم للإفتقار المنافي للوجوب
His being specific to a direction means that He is specific to a space (hayyiz) like the specificity of substances (jawhar), or specific to substances like the specificity of accidents (arad). And the impossibility of His being a substance or accident has become clear (1). Therefore His being specific to a direction is impossible. And if by 'direction' (2) is intended other than these two meanings, it is a mistake in the name with [a correct] meaning? (Footnote 1: Or a body, for space is specific to the substance and the body, and His transcendence, exalted, from them has passed. As for the accident, it has no specificity to space except through being a state in the substance, so it is dependent on the substance's specificity. And since the falsehood of substantiality and corporeality has become clear. (2) Al-Nasafi said in Sharh al-‘Umdah: Forms and directions are different, and their combination upon Allah, exalted, is impossible due to their mutual contradiction, and not some are more deserving than others because all are equal in conferring praise and deficiency, and the lack of indication of created things upon Him. So if He were specific to any of them, it would require a particulariser, and that is one of the signs of the created.' He said: Al-Subki said: The Maker of the world is not in a direction, because if He were in a direction, He would be in a place necessarily, and if He were in a place, He would be extended (mutahayyiz), and if He were extended, He would be in need of His space and place, so He would not be necessary of existence. And it is established that He is necessary of existence, which is contradictory. Also, if He were in a direction, then either in all directions, which is absurd and repugnant, or in some, which necessitates particularisation that entails need, contrary to necessity.)
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and disjunctive argument. It begins by analyzing the concept of 'being specific to a direction' (ikhtisas bi-jiha). This can only mean two things: either being in a space (hayyiz) like a substance, or inhering in a substance like an accident. Since it has been established earlier that Allah is neither a substance nor an accident (nor a body), it follows that He cannot be in a direction. If someone uses 'direction' in a different sense, they are simply misusing language while intending a correct meaning—but the term itself is inappropriate. The first footnote extends the argument to bodies and clarifies that accidents depend on substances. The second footnote quotes al-Nasafi and al-Subki, who provide additional reasoning: directions are mutually contradictory and cannot all apply to Allah; if He were in one direction, it would require a particulariser, a mark of contingency. Al-Subki adds that being in a direction implies being in a place, which implies being extended (mutahayyiz) and thus in need—impossible for the Necessary Being.

واجب الوجود

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Direction can be used for Allah in a metaphorical sense without implying substance or accident.
Correction: Ghazali shows that any literal understanding of direction reduces to either being in space or inhering, both impossible. Metaphorical usage is either meaningless or inconsistent with divine transcendence.

Checkpoint

A) To be in a space like a substance or to inhere like an accident.
B) To be above or below.
C) To have a body.

Key Takeaway
Allah is free from direction because direction would imply being a substance or accident, both impossible for the Necessary Being.

?

?
You recite 'Allahu Akbar' daily. Does the grandeur of His transcendence beyond place and direction truly move your heart? Or have you become comfortable with a God you can mentally locate? Name one instance today where you will explicitly remind yourself of His directionlessness to deepen your awe.

The Impossibility of Allah Being Above the World
Ghazali argues that if Allah were above the world, He would be adjacent and comparable, implying contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that if Allah were spatially above the world, it would imply adjacency, comparability, and contingency, which is impossible for the Creator.

Core Idea
Spatial above implies adjacency, comparability, and need – impossible for Allah.

Imam al-Ghazali presents a powerful argument against anthropomorphism: claiming that Allah is spatially above the world forces Him into relationship with bodies, making Him comparable to them. This passage uses pure logic to show the impossibility of such a view.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the logical flow from 'if above' to 'adjacent' to 'comparable'.
The argument is concise; each conditional step is essential.
2
Identify the chain of implications
List the implications in order: above → adjacent → comparable in size (equal, smaller, larger) → measurement → need for a measurer → impossibility for Allah.
This chain shows that spatial above leads to contingency.
3
Reflect on why measurement implies need
Measurement implies something that determines the size – a measurer. That indicates dependence and contingency, which is impossible for the Self-Sufficient Creator.
Allah is al-Khaliq, the Creator, not created.
4
Connect to divine transcendence
Understand that this argument upholds tanzih (transcendence): Allah is not subject to spatial relations or comparisons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنه لو كان فوق العالم لكان محاذياً له ، وكل محاذ لجسم ، فإما أن يكون مثله أو أصغر أو أكبر ، وكل ذلك تقدير محوج بالضرورة إلى مقدر ، ويتعالى عنه الخالق الواحد المدبر)

(۴) جل جلاله ، وقد ذكر المصنف في كتابه الجام العوام : اعلم أن الفوق إسم مشترك يطلق لمعنيين : أحدهما نسبة جسم إلى جسم بأن يكون أحدهما أعلم يعني الأعلى من جانب رأس الأسفل وقد لا يكون بهذا المعنى فيقال الخليفة فوق السلطان والسلطان فوق الوزير ، والأول يستدعي جسمها حتى ينسب إلى جسم . والثاني لا يستدعيه فليعتقد المؤمن أن الأول غير مراد وأنه على الله تعالى محال فإنه من لوازم الأجسام أو لوازم أعراض الأجسام
And because if He were above the world, He would be adjacent to it, and everything adjacent to a body is either like it, smaller, or larger – and all that is a measurement that necessarily requires a measurer – and the One Creator and Manager is exalted above that. (Footnote: May His glory be exalted. And the author mentioned in his book Iljam al-‘Awamm: Know that 'above' (fawq) is a homonym used for two meanings: one is the relation of a body to a body such that one of them is higher, i.e., the higher from the side of the head of the lower, and it may not be in this sense, so it is said 'the caliph is above the sultan' and 'the sultan is above the vizier'. The first requires them to be bodies in order to be relative to a body. The second does not require that. So let the believer believe that the first is not intended and that it is impossible for Allah, exalted, for it is a necessary concomitant of bodies or of accidents of bodies.)
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument: if Allah were above (fawq) the world, He would be adjacent (muhadhi) to it. Adjacency to a body implies comparability in size—equal, smaller, or larger. All such measurements require a determiner (muqaddir), implying need and contingency, which is impossible for the Self-Sufficient Creator. The footnote, from Imam Ghazali's own work Iljam al-‘Awamm, clarifies that the word 'fawq' is ambiguous. It has a literal physical meaning (one body above another) and a metaphorical meaning (superiority, as in 'the caliph above the sultan'). The physical meaning requires corporeality and is impossible for Allah. The metaphorical meaning is not intended when speaking of Allah's relation to the world. Thus believers must affirm that the literal sense is negated, and any term suggestive of direction is to be understood in a manner befitting Allah's transcendence.

محاذاة
تقدير
مقدر
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that 'above' for Allah can be taken literally as spatial direction.
Correction: This argument shows that spatial above leads to unavoidable comparisons and contingency, which are incompatible with Allah's nature.

Checkpoint

A) Adjacent to it
B) Separate from it
C) Invisible
D) Unknowable

Key Takeaway
The argument demonstrates that any spatial relation for Allah leads to comparability and need, which contradicts His divine nature.

The Two Meanings of 'Above'
Ghazali clarifies the two meanings of 'fawq' and rules out the physical one for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Arabic word 'fawq' has two meanings – physical and metaphorical – and that only the metaphorical applies to Allah.

Core Idea
'Above' is ambiguous: spatial for bodies, metaphorical for authority.

In the footnote from his book Iljam al-‘Awamm, al-Ghazali clarifies a critical linguistic point: the word 'fawq' (above) is not always literal. Understanding this prevents misinterpreting verses about Allah's 'above-ness' and helps maintain tanzih (transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it begins by stating that 'fawq' is a homonym.
The footnote is from Ghazali's own work.
2
Identify the two meanings
Find the two meanings of 'fawq': 1) physical relation between bodies (e.g., one body higher than another), 2) metaphorical superiority (e.g., 'caliph above sultan').
The first meaning requires bodies; the second does not.
3
Understand the examples
Reflect on the examples: 'the caliph is above the sultan' uses 'above' in a non-spatial sense. This shows the second meaning is about rank or authority.
Arabic uses the same word for both.
4
Apply to Allah
Believers must affirm that the first (literal) meaning is not intended for Allah, because it would necessitate corporeality. Only the second meaning of absolute superiority is befitting.
This protects divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولأنه لو كان فوق العالم لكان محاذياً له ، وكل محاذ لجسم ، فإما أن يكون مثله أو أصغر أو أكبر ، وكل ذلك تقدير محوج بالضرورة إلى مقدر ، ويتعالى عنه الخالق الواحد المدبر)

(۴) جل جلاله ، وقد ذكر المصنف في كتابه الجام العوام : اعلم أن الفوق إسم مشترك يطلق لمعنيين : أحدهما نسبة جسم إلى جسم بأن يكون أحدهما أعلم يعني الأعلى من جانب رأس الأسفل وقد لا يكون بهذا المعنى فيقال الخليفة فوق السلطان والسلطان فوق الوزير ، والأول يستدعي جسمها حتى ينسب إلى جسم . والثاني لا يستدعيه فليعتقد المؤمن أن الأول غير مراد وأنه على الله تعالى محال فإنه من لوازم الأجسام أو لوازم أعراض الأجسام
And because if He were above the world, He would be adjacent to it, and everything adjacent to a body is either like it, smaller, or larger – and all that is a measurement that necessarily requires a measurer – and the One Creator and Manager is exalted above that. (Footnote: May His glory be exalted. And the author mentioned in his book Iljam al-‘Awamm: Know that 'above' (fawq) is a homonym used for two meanings: one is the relation of a body to a body such that one of them is higher, i.e., the higher from the side of the head of the lower, and it may not be in this sense, so it is said 'the caliph is above the sultan' and 'the sultan is above the vizier'. The first requires them to be bodies in order to be relative to a body. The second does not require that. So let the believer believe that the first is not intended and that it is impossible for Allah, exalted, for it is a necessary concomitant of bodies or of accidents of bodies.)
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents a third argument: if Allah were above (fawq) the world, He would be adjacent (muhadhi) to it. Adjacency to a body implies comparability in size—equal, smaller, or larger. All such measurements require a determiner (muqaddir), implying need and contingency, which is impossible for the Self-Sufficient Creator. The footnote, from Imam Ghazali's own work Iljam al-‘Awamm, clarifies that the word 'fawq' is ambiguous. It has a literal physical meaning (one body above another) and a metaphorical meaning (superiority, as in 'the caliph above the sultan'). The physical meaning requires corporeality and is impossible for Allah. The metaphorical meaning is not intended when speaking of Allah's relation to the world. Thus believers must affirm that the literal sense is negated, and any term suggestive of direction is to be understood in a manner befitting Allah's transcendence.

Key Terms
فوق
إسم مشترك
نسبة
جسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that since Allah is described as 'above' in the Quran, it must be a literal spatial direction.
Correction: Ghazali explains that 'above' is ambiguous; the literal spatial sense is impossible for Allah, and the metaphorical sense of absolute superiority is what is meant.

Checkpoint
According to the footnote, what does the second meaning of 'fawq' not require?
A) Bodies
B) A direction
C) A relation
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The word 'above' can be used metaphorically; for Allah, only the metaphorical meaning of absolute superiority is correct, not spatial direction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is above everything', do you ever catch yourself imagining a spatial direction? How can you train your heart to think only in terms of absolute authority and transcendence?
?
The first meaning of 'above' requires bodies. Have you ever unconsciously attributed a body to Allah by using such language? What specific phrases will you avoid from now on?
?
After learning that the Quran's 'above' is metaphorical for authority, how does this change your understanding of Allah's nearness? What verse will you reflect on tonight to reinforce this?

The Necessity of Ta'wil for Istiwa
Ghazali cites poetic evidence for ta'wil of Istiwa and compares to ta'wil of 'He is with you'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali uses poetic evidence for the interpretation of Istiwa as Istila and how this ta'wil is analogous to the accepted interpretation of 'He is with you.'

Core Idea
Ta'wil of divine attributes is necessary to avoid anthropomorphism, as shown by linguistic precedent.

Ghazali presents a key argument for metaphorical interpretation (ta'wil) of the Qur'anic attribute of Istiwa (establishment). He cites a line of poetry where the word is used to mean 'domination' without physical contact, and draws a parallel to the universally accepted interpretation of another verse. This lesson explores how linguistic evidence and logical necessity compel the people of truth to adopt such interpretations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the poetic evidence
Focus on the poet's line: 'Bishr has become istawa over Iraq without sword or spilled blood.' Notice how 'istawa' here means 'dominated' or 'gained control over' without physical contact. This shows the word can carry the meaning of istila.
3
Recognize the analogy to the 'Ma'akum' verse
Ghazali compares the ta'wil of 'istiwa' to the ta'wil of 'He is with you wherever you are'. Just as the people of falsehood were compelled to interpret 'with you' as encompassing knowledge, the people of truth are compelled to interpret 'istiwa' as domination. This analogy shows that ta'wil is necessary and not arbitrary.
Both interpretations avoid attributing physical location to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والإستيلاء كما قال الشاعر: قد استوى بشر على العراق من غير سيف ودم مهراق واضطر أهل الحق إلى هذا التأويل كما اضطر أهل الباطل إلى تأويل قوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ مَعَكُمْ أَيْنَمَا كُنتُمْ﴾ إذ حمل ذلك بالاتفاق على الإحاطة والعلم
And al-istila (domination) as the poet said: 'Bishr has become istawa over Iraq without sword or spilled blood.' And the people of truth were compelled to this ta'wil just as the people of falsehood were compelled to the ta'wil of His statement, the Exalted: 'And He is with you wherever you are'—they interpreted that by consensus as encompassing and knowledge.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage defines the interpretation of the attribute of istiwa (establishment) as istila (domination/conquest). The author begins by citing a poet's line where 'istawa' is used to mean 'dominated' or 'gained control over' without warfare, proving that the word can carry this meaning in Arabic. He then notes that the people of truth (Ahl al-Haqq) were compelled to adopt this metaphorical interpretation for the verse about Allah's istiwa on the Throne, just as the people of falsehood (Ahl al-Batil) were compelled to interpret the verse 'He is with you wherever you are' as referring to Allah's encompassing knowledge, not His physical presence. Both interpretations are necessary to avoid anthropomorphism, and there is consensus on the meaning of the latter verse.

Key Terms
الاستواء
الاستيلاء
التأويل
الإحاطة
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ta'wil of divine attributes is optional or innovated.
Correction: This passage shows that ta'wil is necessary and analogous to accepted interpretations of other verses, like 'He is with you' being understood as knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'اضطر أهل الحق إلى هذا التأويل'?
A) They were forced by opponents to interpret it.
B) They were compelled by reason and revelation to interpret it metaphorically.
C) They were forced by rulers to change the meaning.
D) They had no choice but to reject the verse.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of ta'wil for divine attributes is supported by linguistic evidence and analogy to universally accepted interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the verse about Allah's istiwa on the Throne, do you feel a tension between the apparent meaning and your belief in tanzih? How does this lesson's argument for ta'wil change your understanding, or does it create new questions in your heart?
?
The poet's usage of 'istawa' to mean 'dominated' is from a secular context. Does accepting this ta'wil for Allah make you uncomfortable because it seems to rely on non-religious speech? If so, what does that reveal about your assumptions about the Qur'an's language?
?
The lesson draws an analogy to the ta'wil of 'He is with you' which is universally accepted. Have you ever thought about other verses that might require similar ta'wil? What would it mean for you personally to apply this principle consistently to all of Allah's attributes?

Metaphorical Interpretation of Prophetic Hadiths on Divine Attributes
Ghazali interprets two hadiths metaphorically and provides a logical justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ghazali interprets these two hadiths metaphorically and the logical principle behind such interpretation.

Core Idea
Literal reading of divine attributes leads to impossibility, so metaphorical interpretation is necessary.

In this passage, Ghazali applies the method of ta'wil to two well-known hadiths that seem to attribute physical features to Allah. He shows that taking them literally would imply impossible consequences, thus confirming that they must be understood as metaphors for divine power and honor.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the English translation. Let the author's style come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ghazali uses a concise, logical style.
2
Identify the two hadiths and their interpretations
Find the two hadiths mentioned: 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the Most Merciful' and 'The Black Stone is the right hand of Allah on His earth'. Note how Ghazali interprets each one: first as power and domination, second as honor and veneration.
The interpretations are given right after each hadith.
3
Understand the logical argument
Examine Ghazali's argument: if the literal meaning is taken, it would imply that Allah's attributes involve physical contact and occupancy of space, leading to impossibilities such as equality or comparison with accidents. Therefore, metaphorical interpretation is necessary.
This argument is based on the principle that what leads to impossibility is itself impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحمل قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: قلب المؤمن بين أصبعين من أصابع الرحمن على القدرة والقهر، وحمل قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم: الحجر الأسود يمين الله في أرضه على التشريف والإكرام، لأنه لو ترك على ظاهره للزم منه المحال، كون المتمكن جسما مماساً لو ترك على الإستقرار والتمكن لزم الإستواء للعرض، اما مثله أو أكبر منه أو أصغر، وذلك محال، وما يؤدي إلى المحال فهو محال
And he interpreted his statement, peace be upon him: 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the fingers of the Most Merciful' as [referring to] power and domination. And he interpreted his statement, peace be upon him: 'The Black Stone is the right hand of Allah on His earth' as honor and veneration. Because if it were left on its apparent meaning, it would lead to impossibility from it: the thing established becomes a body touching, if left on settling and occupying space, it would necessitate istiwa for the accident, either being like it or larger or smaller, and that is impossible, and what leads to impossibility is itself impossible.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage applies the principle of ta'wil to two specific hadiths. First, the hadith 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the Most Merciful' is interpreted metaphorically as referring to divine power and domination (qahr), not literal fingers. Second, the hadith 'The Black Stone is the right hand of Allah on His earth' is interpreted as a symbol of honor and veneration (tashrif wa ikram), not a literal hand. The author then gives a brief logical justification: if the apparent meaning were taken literally, it would imply that Allah's attributes involve physical contact and occupancy of space, which would lead to impossibilities such as Allah being equal to, larger, or smaller than the accident (al-ard). This would be impossible for Allah, and anything that leads to impossibility is itself impossible; hence, metaphorical interpretation is necessary.

Key Terms
الأصابع
اليمين
القدرة
القهر
التشريف
الإكرام
المحال
المتمكن
التمكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These hadiths should be taken literally because they are from the Prophet.
Correction: Ghazali argues that literal interpretation leads to impossible consequences for Allah, so metaphorical interpretation is necessary.

Checkpoint
Why does the author interpret 'الحجر الأسود يمين الله' metaphorically?
A) Because the Black Stone is not actually a stone.
B) Because a literal hand would attribute a body part to Allah, leading to impossibility.
C) Because the hadith is weak.
D) Because the Prophet meant it as a joke.

Key Takeaway
Ghazali interprets hadiths on divine attributes metaphorically to avoid attributing physicality to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the hadith 'The heart of the believer is between two fingers of the Most Merciful,' does your heart immediately recoil from the literal image of fingers, or do you find yourself tempted to visualize something physical? What does that reaction teach you about the state of your tawhid?
?
If you were to explain Ghazali's justification—that a literal reading leads to impossibility—to someone who insists on a literal interpretation, would you feel confident in your own grasp of the argument? What would it take for you to internalize this logical defense so deeply that it shapes your immediate response to ambiguous hadiths?
?
This lesson shows that even prophetic words must be interpreted in a way that upholds Allah's transcendence. Are there any beliefs or practices you hold about Allah that, upon honest reflection, might actually diminish His majesty? Will you commit to examining one such belief today and seeking the correct understanding from the scholars of Ahl al-Sunnah?

The Threefold Impossibility of Allah Occupying Space
Ghazali enumerates a logical trilemma against spatial divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical trilemma that Ghazali uses to prove that Allah cannot settle on or face any place.

Core Idea
If Allah were in a place, He would be equal, larger, or smaller — all impossible.

Imam al-Ghazali presents a precise logical argument to demonstrate that describing Allah with spatial attributes leads to absurd conclusions. He shows that any claim of Allah occupying a place forces one into a three-way impossibility, each contradicting divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, noting the structure of the argument.
The passage begins with 'وتحقيق ذلك أنه تعالى لو إستقر على مكان...'
2
Identify the three cases
List the three possibilities Ghazali enumerates for Allah's relationship to a place: equal, larger, or smaller. Understand why each leads to impossibility.
The trilemma is exhaustive — no fourth possibility exists.
3
Reflect on each impossibility
For each case, consider the theological consequence: equality implies shape, larger implies division, smaller implies limitation.
All three contradict divine simplicity and transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحقيق ذلك أنه تعالى لو إستقر على مكان أو حاذى مكاناً لم يخل من أن يكون مثل المكان أو أكبر منه أو أصغر منه. فإن كان مثل المكان فهو إذا متشكل بأشكال المكان حتى إذا كان المكان مربعاً مربعاً، أو كان مثلنا كان هو مثلنا وذلك محال وإن كان أكبر من المكان فبعضه على المكان ويشعر ذلك بأنه متجزىء وله كل ينطوي على بعض، وكان بحيث ينتسب إليه المكان بأنه ربعه أو خمسه، وإن كان أصغر من ذلك المكان بقدر لم يتميز عن ذلك المكان إلا بتحديد، وتتطرق إليه المساحة والتقدير، وكل ما يؤدي إلى جواز التقدير على الباري تعالى، فتجوزه في حقه كفر من معتقده وكل من جاز عليه الكون بذاته إنه على محل لم يتميز عن ذلك المحال ألا يكون وقبيح وصف الباري بالكون، ومتى جاز عليه موازاة مكان أو مماسته جاز عليه مباينته، ومن جاز عليه المباينة =
And the verification of that is that He, the Exalted, if He settled on a place or faced a place, would not be free from being like the place, or larger than it, or smaller than it. If He were like the place, then He would be conforming to the shapes of the place such that if the place is square, He is square; or if He is like us, then He is like us—and that is impossible. And if He were larger than the place, then part of Him would be on the place, and that indicates that He is divisible and has parts that encompass each other, and such that the place would be related to Him as a quarter or a fifth. And if He were smaller than that place by an amount, He would not be distinguished from that place except by limitation, and measurement and estimation would apply to Him. And everything that leads to allowing measurement of Allah, the Exalted—to allow it regarding Him is kufr from the one who believes it. And whoever considers it permissible for Him to be in Himself in a place, he does not distinguish that place from that impossibility... and it is ugly to describe Allah with spatial location. And whenever facing a place or touching it is permissible for Him, then being separate from it is permissible for Him, and whoever permits separate-ness...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical proof (tahqiq) demonstrating the impossibility of Allah occupying space or having physical dimensions. The author presents a trilemma: If Allah were to settle on or face a place, He must either be equal to the place in size, larger, or smaller. Each case leads to absurd consequences. If equal, then Allah would conform to the shape of the place, implying that He is like physical objects, which is impossible. If larger, then only part of Him would be on the place, implying division and parts, which contradicts divine simplicity. If smaller, then He would be limited and measurable, subject to spatial dimensions. Allowing measurement (taqdir) of Allah is considered kufr. The author concludes that describing Allah with spatial location (kawn) is repugnant, and if physical contact or adjacency were possible, then separation would also be possible, which further leads to unacceptable consequences. The argument is cut off at the end of the page but is a standard kalam proof for divine transcendence.

Key Terms
الاستقرار
المكان
المحاذاة
المثل
الأكبر
الأصغر
التشكل
التجزؤ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if Allah is 'above the Throne' literally, He must be in a place like a body.
Correction: Ghazali's trilemma shows that any spatial location leads to absurdities incompatible with divine perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what would be entailed if Allah were larger than the place He occupies?
A) He would be omnipresent.
B) He would have parts and be divisible.
C) He would be equal to the place in shape.
D) He would be smaller than the place.

Key Takeaway
Any literal occupation of space by Allah leads to one of three impossibilities: shape, division, or limitation—all contradict divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read 'settled on a place,' do you find yourself unconsciously imagining Allah in a specific location? How does the trilemma expose that assumption as a limitation you placed on the Creator, and what will you actively do to train your mind to reject that image?
?
If you have ever felt that Allah is 'close' in a spatial sense (like physically near you), how does this lesson purify your understanding of His nearness? What concrete change in your perception of closeness will you make starting today?
?
What subtle comfort do you derive from imagining Allah in a place—perhaps a sense of control or tangibility? Are you willing to let go of that comfort to uphold His absolute transcendence, even if it feels unsettling? What prayer can you make to seek help in that surrender?

The Blasphemy of Measuring Allah
Ghazali concludes by declaring measurement of Allah as kufr and linking spatial location to separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why allowing any form of measurement or spatial location for Allah constitutes disbelief and why even the possibility of physical adjacency leads to unacceptable consequences.

Core Idea
Allowing any measurement of Allah is kufr, and spatial location implies division and limitation.

After the trilemma, Ghazali drives home the severe theological stakes. He declares that anyone who allows measurement (taqdir) of Allah has committed disbelief. He further argues that permitting spatial location leads to ugly descriptions and eventually to implications of separateness (mubayana), which are also impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the conclusion passage
Read the Arabic from 'وكل ما يؤدي' to the end, and the corresponding translation.
The text is cut off, but the main points are clear.
2
Understand the ruling of kufr
Notice how Ghazali states that allowing measurement (taqdir) on Allah is kufr. Reflect on why this is so severe.
Measurement implies finitude and composition, incompatible with divine perfection.
3
Connect to the impossibility of separation
Understand the final argument: if physical adjacency (muwazah or mumassa) were possible, then separation (mubayana) would also be possible, leading to absurdities.
The argument is symmetrical: spatial relations imply separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتحقيق ذلك أنه تعالى لو إستقر على مكان أو حاذى مكاناً لم يخل من أن يكون مثل المكان أو أكبر منه أو أصغر منه. فإن كان مثل المكان فهو إذا متشكل بأشكال المكان حتى إذا كان المكان مربعاً مربعاً، أو كان مثلنا كان هو مثلنا وذلك محال وإن كان أكبر من المكان فبعضه على المكان ويشعر ذلك بأنه متجزىء وله كل ينطوي على بعض، وكان بحيث ينتسب إليه المكان بأنه ربعه أو خمسه، وإن كان أصغر من ذلك المكان بقدر لم يتميز عن ذلك المكان إلا بتحديد، وتتطرق إليه المساحة والتقدير، وكل ما يؤدي إلى جواز التقدير على الباري تعالى، فتجوزه في حقه كفر من معتقده وكل من جاز عليه الكون بذاته إنه على محل لم يتميز عن ذلك المحال ألا يكون وقبيح وصف الباري بالكون، ومتى جاز عليه موازاة مكان أو مماسته جاز عليه مباينته، ومن جاز عليه المباينة =

If He were like the place, then He would be conforming to the shapes of the place such that if the place is square, He is square; or if He is like us, then He is like us—and that is impossible. And if He were larger than the place, then part of Him would be on the place, and that indicates that He is divisible and has parts that encompass each other, and such that the place would be related to Him as a quarter or a fifth. And everything that leads to allowing measurement of Allah, the Exalted—to allow it regarding Him is kufr from the one who believes it. And whenever facing a place or touching it is permissible for Him, then being separate from it is permissible for Him, and whoever permits separate-ness...
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a detailed logical proof (tahqiq) demonstrating the impossibility of Allah occupying space or having physical dimensions. The author presents a trilemma: If Allah were to settle on or face a place, He must either be equal to the place in size, larger, or smaller. Each case leads to absurd consequences. The Ninth Principle: Vision of Allah in the Hereafter
Ghazali affirms ru'ya in the Hereafter and denies it in this world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah will be seen by the believers in the Hereafter despite His transcendence, and that vision is impossible in this world.

Core Idea
Allah is seen in the Hereafter, not in this world.

This lesson introduces the ninth principle of Islamic creed regarding the beatific vision (ru'ya). Imam Ghazali explains that although Allah is absolutely transcendent beyond form and direction, He will be visible to the believers in the afterlife. This is a matter of transmitted knowledge (sam'iyyat), proven by Quranic verses. Let us examine his concise formulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic text once, letting its rhythm sink in. Then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Notice the careful balance between transcendence (tanzih) and visibility.
2
Identify the two key claims
The passage makes two related claims: (1) Allah is seen in the Hereafter, (2) He is not seen in this world. Identify the proof verses for each.
The proof for vision is from Surah al-Qiyamah; the denial is from Surah al-An'am.
3
Understand the doctrinal nuance
Reflect on why vision in the Hereafter does not compromise Allah's transcendence. The passage explicitly states His freedom from form and direction, yet affirms visibility—indicating a special kind of vision.
This is a key point in Sunni creed: vision without physicality.
4
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question. It directly checks if you remember when vision occurs.
5
Summarize in your own words
Write a one-sentence summary of the principle as Ghazali presents it. This will reinforce the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل التاسع : الرؤية : العلم بأنه تعالى مع كونه منزها عن الصورة والمقدار مقدسا عن الجهات والأقطار ، مرئي بالأعين والأبصار في الدار الآخرة دار القرار ، لقوله تعالى : وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرةً ، إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ . ولا يرى في الدنيا تصديقاً لقوله عز وجل : لا تُدْرِكُهُ الأبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الْأَبْصَارَ
The ninth principle: al-Ru'ya: The knowledge that He, exalted, while being free from form and measurement, holy from directions and poles, is visible to the eyes and sights in the abode of the Hereafter, the abode of permanence, according to His saying: 'Faces on that day are radiant, looking at their Lord.' And He is not seen in this world, confirming His saying, mighty and majestic: 'Sights do not perceive Him, and He perceives sights.'
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage defines the ninth principle of Aqidah regarding the beatific vision (ru'ya). It begins by asserting that Allah is transcendent (munazzah) beyond form, measurement, directions, and poles. Despite this transcendence, He will be seen by the believers in the Hereafter. The proof is the Quranic verse from Surah al-Qiyamah where faces are described as 'looking at their Lord' (nazira). This verse is interpreted literally as vision. However, vision in this world is denied by the verse from Surah al-An'am: 'Sights do not perceive Him.' Thus, the principle establishes both the possibility of vision in the afterlife and its impossibility in this world.

Key Terms

الدار الآخرة
تنزيه (tanzih)
لا تدركه الأبصار

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage affirms vision in the Hereafter, so invisibility is only for this world, not absolute.

Allah will be seen by the believers in the Hereafter, but not in this world.
