The Black Stone Hadith: Literal Interpretation Leads to Impossibility
Ghazali uses the Black Stone hadith to illustrate the principle of interpreting anthropomorphic expressions metaphorically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing physical contact to Allah (like the Black Stone as His right hand) is impossible and how to interpret such hadith correctly.

Core Idea
Taking anthropomorphic hadith literally implies impossibility, so they must be interpreted metaphorically.

The Prophet's saying that the Black Stone is the right hand of God on earth seems to imply physical contact. But Ghazali explains that if taken literally, it forces us to imagine Allah as a body touching a stone, which is impossible. Instead, the hadith is a metaphor for honor and veneration. This lesson clarifies the logical reasoning behind rejecting anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to see the hadith and its two possible interpretations.
2
Identify the two impossible consequences
Notice that Ghazali lists two impossibilities: being a body in contact, and istiwa requiring equality or disparity.
3
Understand the principle
Grasp the key rule: whatever leads to impossibility is itself impossible, so the literal meaning must be rejected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقهر، وحمل قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم : « الحجر الأسود يمين الله في أرضه على فكذا التشريف والإكرام، لأنه لو ترك على ظاهره للزم منه المحال، كون المتمكن جسما مماساً لو ترك على الإستقرار والتمكن لزم الإستواء، للعرض، اما مثله أو أكبر منه أو أصغر، وذلك محال، وما يؤدي إلى المحال فهو محال. والمماسة، لم يكن إلا حادثاً، وهل علمنا حدوث العالم إلا بجواز المماسة والمباينة على إجزائه، وقصارى الجهلة قولهم: كيف يتصور موجود لافي محل، وهذه الكلمة تصدر عن بدع وغوائل لا يعرف غورها وقعرها إلا كل غواص على بحار الحقائق، وهيهات طلب الكيفية حيث يستحيل محال والذي يدحض شبههم أن يقال لهم قبل أن يخلق العالم، أو المكان هل كان موجوداً أم لا، فمن ضرورة العقل أن يقول بلى، فيستلزمه لو صح قوله لا يعلم موجوداً إلا في مكان أحد أمرين: إما أن يقول المكان والعرش والعالم قديم، وإما أن يقول الله تعالى محدث، وهذا مآل الجهلة والحشوية، ليس القديم بالمحدث والمحدث بالقديم، ونعوذ بالله من الحيرة في الدين يقول إبن الهمام في المسايرة، وعلى نحو ما ذكرنا في الإستواء يجري كل ما ورد في الكتاب والسنة مما ظاهره الجسمية في الشاهد كالأصبع والقدم واليد والعين، فيجب الإيمان به مصحوباً بالتنزيه فإن كلا منها صفة له تعالى لا بمعنى الجارحة بل على وجه يليق به فهو سبحانه وتعالى أعلم به
And overpowering, and carrying his saying, peace be upon him: 'The Black Stone is the right hand of God in His earth' upon honor and veneration, because if it were left upon its apparent meaning, impossibility would necessarily follow: that the established being is a body touching, if left upon settlement and establishment, establishment (al-istiwa) would require, for the accident, either like it or larger or smaller, and that is impossible, and what leads to the impossible is impossible. And touching and contact would not be except as originated. And have we known the origination of the world except by the permissibility of contact and separation upon its parts? And the utmost of the ignorant is their saying: 'How can an existent be conceived without a place?' And this word issues from innovations and evils whose depth and bottom no one knows except every diver into the seas of truths. How far is the seeking of how-ness where impossibility is impossible? And what refutes their doubt is that it is said to them: Before the creation of the world, were the world or place existent or not? Reason necessarily says: Yes. Then it would necessitate, if his statement 'no existent is known except in a place' were correct, one of two things: either to say that place, the Throne, and the world are eternal, or to say that God is originated. And this is the fate of the ignorant and the anthropomorphists. The eternal is not originated, and the originated is not eternal. And we seek refuge in God from confusion in religion. Ibn al-Humam says in al-Musayara: And according to what we mentioned regarding al-istiwa, everything that came in the Book and Sunnah whose apparent meaning is corporeality in the witnessed world, like the finger, foot, hand, and eye, is to be treated: it is obligatory to believe in them accompanied by transcendence (tanzih), for each of them is an attribute of Him, the Exalted, not in the sense of a limb, but in a manner befitting Him. He is glorified and exalted and knows best.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive refutation of anthropomorphism (tashbih) and spatial location for Allah. It begins with the example of the hadith about the Black Stone, explaining that the phrase 'right hand of God' is metaphorical, meaning honor and veneration. If taken literally, it would imply Allah has a body and is in contact, which leads to the impossible (muhal). Then it addresses the issue of istiwa (establishing on the Throne): if Allah were established in a place, He would have to be equal to, larger than, or smaller than that place, all of which are impossible for the Creator. The passage then argues that spatial contact and separation are signs of originatedness (huduth), and the world's origination is known through the possibility of contact and separation among its parts. Ignorants ask how an existent can be without place; this question itself stems from innovation. The refutation is that before the creation of the world and place, Allah existed alone, so it is not necessary for existence to be in a place. If one insists that every existent must be in a place, then either place is eternal (which is false) or Allah is originated (which is false). Thus, the only correct position is to believe in Allah's transcendence. Finally, the passage concludes with a general rule: all expressions in Quran and Sunnah that appear corporeal (like hand, face, etc.) must be affirmed as attributes of Allah, but without meaning physical limbs, interpreted in a manner befitting His majesty. This balances affirmation (ithbat) and transcendence (tanzih).

Key Terms
الحجر الأسود
يمين الله
تمكن (tamakkun)
استواء (istiwa)
محال (muhal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Black Stone is literally God's right hand
Correction: Ghazali explains it is a metaphor for honor and veneration; literal meaning leads to impossibility.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the correct interpretation of the hadith 'The Black Stone is the right hand of God'?
A) It means God literally has a hand touching the stone
B) It is a metaphor for honor and veneration
C) It means God is physically present in the stone
D) It is not a valid hadith

Key Takeaway
The Black Stone hadith must be interpreted metaphorically because a literal reading would lead to impossible implications about Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself insisting on a literal understanding of a religious text about Allah, even when it led to an irrational mental image? How can you train yourself to embrace metaphorical interpretations when they protect Allah's transcendence?
?
If you truly believe that Allah is beyond physical attributes, why do you sometimes catch yourself imagining Him in human-like terms? What does that reveal about your reliance on worldly concepts, and how can you purify your understanding?
?
The Black Stone is a revered object, but the hadith reminds us not to confuse veneration with divinity. Are there people, places, or things in your life that you have unknowingly elevated to a status that belongs only to Allah? How can you recalibrate your heart's reverence to be solely for Him?

Refuting Spatial Location: The Logical Dilemma and the Principle of Tanzih
Ghazali presents a systematic refutation of spatial location and lays down the principle for interpreting bodily attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that Allah cannot be in a place, and how to correctly interpret all corporeal-sounding attributes in the Quran and Sunnah.

Core Idea
Spatial contact implies origination, and the claim that every existent must be in a place leads to an impossible choice.

Ghazali extends his argument to any notion of spatial location for Allah. He shows that contact and separation are signs of createdness, so the Creator must be free of them. Then he refutes the ignorant objection that an existent cannot be without place by presenting a logical dilemma: either place is eternal or Allah is originated, both false. Finally, he provides a general rule for interpreting all similar attributes: affirm them with tanzih.

Guided Path
1
Read the continuation of the passage
Read from 'And touching and contact would not be except as originated' to the end to see the full argument.
2
Analyze the argument about contact and origination
Understand why the possibility of contact among parts of the world indicates they are created, and thus the Creator cannot have contact.
3
Examine the logical dilemma
The ignorant claim that every existent is in a place leads to either place being eternal (false) or Allah being originated (false).
4
Learn the general rule for interpreting attributes
All apparent corporeal attributes in the Quran and Sunnah must be affirmed as true attributes of Allah, while maintaining absolute transcendence.

The Principle of Seeing Allah in the Afterlife
Ghazali defines the ninth principle of vision and cites Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni belief that Allah will be seen by believers in the afterlife, and that this is not possible in this world, as affirmed by Quranic evidence.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah in the afterlife is a Quranic promise, not a physical vision.

Imam al-Ghazali presents the ninth principle of Sunni creed: the beatific vision. He clarifies that while Allah is transcendent beyond physical attributes, He will be seen by believers in the Hereafter. This lesson examines the two Quranic proofs he cites to establish this doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: definition, proof for afterlife, proof for denial in this world.
The passage uses two Quranic verses as evidence.
2
Identify the two Quranic proofs
Find the verse from Surah al-Qiyama (75:22-23) that proves vision in the afterlife, and the verse from Surah al-An'am (6:103) that denies it in this world.
These verses are the core evidence.
3
Understand the theological nuance
Reflect on how Ghazali balances divine transcendence (free from form, direction) with the affirmation of being seen. The vision is real but not physical.
This is a key Sunni belief distinguishing from other sects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And He is not seen in this world, affirming His saying: 'Vision does not perceive Him, and He perceives all vision.'

Commentary
This passage defines the ninth principle of the creed, which is the beatific vision (ru'ya). It states that Allah, while being transcendent beyond physical attributes like form, magnitude, and direction, will be seen by the believers in the afterlife. The commentary explains that this is a fundamental Sunni belief.

Allah's Transcendence of Place and Direction
Refutes the necessity of place/direction for Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for Allah to occupy a place or direction and how to interpret anthropomorphic verses correctly.

Core Idea
Allah is not in a place; claiming otherwise leads to absurdity.

The claim that every existent must occupy a place is a common fallacy. In this passage, Imam Ghazali refutes it by showing that it would imply either the eternity of place (false) or the creation of Allah (impossible). He then provides the correct methodology for interpreting scriptural descriptions of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and the logical flow.
2
Identify the two absurd consequences
The passage presents a dilemma: if every existent must be in a place, then before creation either place is eternal (which is false) or Allah is originated (also false). Find these two possibilities.
This dilemma is the core of the refutation.
3
Understand how the argument proves transcendence
By showing that the ignorants' claim leads to impossible conclusions, the passage demonstrates that Allah is not in a place. Reflect on why this is rationally necessary.
4
Learn the principle for interpreting anthropomorphic verses
The passage ends with Ibn al-Humam's principle: affirm the scriptural descriptions while negating any physical meaning. Understand how this preserves both the text and transcendence.
This principle applies to attributes like hand, eye, foot.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمماسة ، لم يكن إلا حادثاً ، وهل علمنا حدوث العالم إلا بجواز المماسة والمباينة على إجزائه ، وقصارى الجهلة قولهم : كيف يتصور موجود لافي محل ، وهذه الكلمة تصدر عن بدع وغوائل لا يعرف غورها وقعرها إلا كل غوّاص على بحار الحقائق ، وهيهات طلب الكيفية حيث يستحيل محال. والذي يدحض شبههم أن يقال لهم قبل أن يخلق العالم ، أو المكان هل كان موجوداً أم لا ، فمن ضرورة العقل أن يقول بلى ، فيستلزمه لو صح قوله لا يعلم موجوداً إلا في مكان أحد أمرين : إما أن يقول المكان والعرش والعالم قديم، وإما أن يقول الله تعالى محدث ، وهذا مآل الجهلة والحشوية ، ليس القديم بالمحدث والمحدث بالقديم ، ونعوذ بالله من الحيرة في الدين يقول إبن الهمام في المسايرة ، وعلى نحو ما ذكرنا في الإستواء يجري كل ما ورد في الكتاب والسنة مما ظاهره الجسمية في الشاهد كالأصبع والقدم واليد والعين ، فيجب الإيمان به مصحوباً بالتنزيه فإن كلا منها صفة له تعالى لا بمعنى الجارحة بل على وجه يليق به فهو سبحانه وتعالى أعلم به
And contact, it would not be except originated. And have we known the origination of the world except by the permissibility of contact and separation upon its parts? The extent of the ignorant is their saying: 'How can an existent be conceived without a place?' And this statement issues from innovations and calamities whose depth and bottom none knows except every diver in the seas of realities. How far is the search for modality where it is impossible, impossible. And that which refutes their doubt is to say to them: Before Allah created the world, was place existent or not? By necessity of the intellect they say yes. Then if his statement that nothing exists except in a place were true, it would entail one of two things: either he says that place, the Throne, and the world are eternal, or he says that Allah is originated. And this is the outcome of the ignorant and the Hashwiyya. The Eternal is not originated and the originated is not eternal. And we seek refuge in Allah from confusion in religion. Ibn al-Humam says in al-Musayara: 'And in the manner we mentioned regarding al-istiwa, every statement in the Book and Sunnah that apparently implies corporeality in the empirical world, like the finger, foot, hand, and eye, must be believed in while accompanied by transcendence. For each of them is an attribute of Him, not in the sense of a limb, but in a manner befitting Him. He, glorified and exalted, knows best.'
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that every existent must occupy a place. The author argues that if place were necessary for existence, then before creation, since no place existed, Allah would have been non-existent, which is absurd. He shows that the only alternatives are that place is eternal (which is false) or that Allah is created (which is false). Then he cites Ibn al-Humam's principle for interpreting anthropomorphic verses: affirm them while denying any physical meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Every existent must occupy a place or direction.
Correction: Allah exists without a place; the claim leads to absurdities such as place being eternal or Allah being created.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what two possibilities arise if we accept the claim that every existent must be in a place?
A) Place is eternal or Allah is created
B) Allah is everywhere or nowhere
C) The world is uncreated or Allah is a body
D) Time is infinite or Allah is temporal

Key Takeaway
Allah is not bound by place or direction; any scriptural expression implying physicality must be understood in a manner befitting His majesty.

?

Proof from Moses' Request for the Beatific Vision
Proves the possibility of seeing Allah from Moses' request and the conditional response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the incident of Moses' request to see Allah provides rational evidence for the possibility of beatific vision, and how the conditional nature of the response supports this.

Core Idea
Moses would not ask for the impossible, so vision must be possible.

The Mu'tazilites argued that seeing Allah is impossible, citing the verse 'You will not see Me' addressed to Moses. But in this passage, the author turns the same incident into a proof for the possibility of vision. By examining both Moses' prophetic knowledge and the conditional structure of Allah's response, we uncover a powerful rational argument that refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two distinct arguments presented.
The passage begins with a rhetorical criticism of the Mu'tazilites.
2
Understand the first argument: Moses' request
Why does the author argue that Moses' request itself proves possibility? Think about the ma'sum (infallibility) of prophets.
Prophets know Allah's attributes, so they cannot ask for something impossible.
3
Understand the second argument: conditional response
Examine how Allah conditioned the vision on the mountain's stability. This conditional phrase indicates possibility, not impossibility.
The mountain's stability was possible, so the vision is possible.
4
Reflect on the counterargument
How does the author refute the idea that 'You will not see Me' is an absolute negation? Consider the distinction between eternal and conditional negation.
The passage argues that the negation is conditional, not absolute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقوله تعالى في خطاب موسى عليه السلام : ﴿ لَنْ تَرَانِي ﴾ وليت شعري كيف عرف المعتزلي من صفات رب الأرباب ما جهله موسى عليه السلام ؟ وكيف سأل موسی عليه السلام الرؤية مع كونها محالا ولعل الجهل يذوي البدع والأهواء من الجهلة الأغبياء أولى من الجهل بالأنبياء صلوات الله عليهم . وحاصل هذا الإستدلال أن سؤال موسى عليه السلام إياها دليل على أنه كان يعتقد أنه كان جائز الرؤية والوجه الثاني أنه تعالى علق الرؤية بشرط منصور الكون وهو إستقرار الجبل ، فدل على أنه جائز الوجود إذ تعليق الفعل بما هو جائز الوجود ، يدل على جوازه ، كما أن التعليق بما هو ممتنع الوجود أو متحقق الوجود يدل على إقناعه ، أو تحققه ، والدليل على أن إستقرار الجبل ممكن الثبوت قوله تعالى : ( فَلَمَّا تَجَلَّى رَبُّهُ لِلْجَبَل جَعَلَهُ دَكّاً ) : أخبر أنه جعله دكا ، لا أنه أندل بنفسه ، وما أوجده الله تعالى كان جائزاً أن لا يوجد لو لم يوجده الله تعالى ، إذ الله تعالى مختار فيما يفعل فإذا جعل الجبل دكا باختياره ، وكان جائزاً أن لا يفعل دل على جواز وجوده .
And due to His saying in addressing Moses, peace be upon him: 'You will not see Me.' Would that I knew how the Mu'tazilite knows from the attributes of the Lord of lords what Moses, peace be upon him, was ignorant of? And how did Moses ask for the vision while it is impossible? Perhaps ignorance in innovations and whims from the ignorant fools is more appropriate than ignorance of the prophets, peace be upon them. The summary of this argument is that Moses' request for it is evidence that he believed it was permissible to see. The second aspect is that He, exalted, conditioned the vision with a condition of support, which is the stability of the mountain, so it indicates that it is possible of existence, for conditioning an action upon what is possible indicates its permissibility, just as conditioning upon what is impossible or actual indicates its impossibility or actuality. The evidence that the mountain's stability is possible of affirmation is His saying: 'When his Lord manifested to the mountain, He made it level.' He informed that He made it level, not that it collapsed by itself. And what Allah created was possible not to exist if Allah had not created it. For Allah is free in what He does. So when He made the mountain level by His choice, and it was possible that He not do so, it indicates the possibility of its existence.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof for the possibility of seeing Allah from the incident in Surah al-A'raf where Moses asks to see Allah. The author argues that Moses, being a prophet, would not ask for something impossible, so vision must be possible. The second argument is that Allah conditioned the vision on the mountain's stability, which is possible, indicating that vision itself is possible. The passage also refutes the Mu'tazilites who deny the vision.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the verse 'You will not see Me' (lan tarani) proves that Allah cannot be seen at all, in this life or the hereafter.
Correction: The passage shows that this negation is conditional and temporary, not an absolute denial of the possibility of seeing Allah in the hereafter. The condition (mountain's stability) is possible, so the vision is possible.

D) Because Allah granted it

Key Takeaway
Moses' request and the conditional nature of Allah's response provide two rational arguments that beatific vision is possible, refuting the Mu'tazilite denial.

?

?

Animal Crafts as Proof of Divine Creation
Ghazali argues that intricate animal productions show God alone is the true creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ghazali uses the intricate productions of animals to argue that only Allah is the true creator, and why animals cannot be independent inventors.

Core Idea
Animals create without knowing what they do, so their Creator must be All-Knowing.

Ghazali presents a powerful argument from the natural world: spiders and bees create intricate structures without any knowledge of the details. This observation leads to a logical conclusion that only Allah, the All-Knowing, can be the true inventor. In this lesson, we will examine Ghazali's reasoning and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question and exclamation.
2
Identify the argument structure
Notice how Ghazali uses a reductio ad absurdum: animals produce intricate crafts but lack knowledge of the details, so they cannot be independent creators. The only logical conclusion is that Allah is the true creator.
The exclamation 'haitahait' emphasizes impossibility.
3
Reflect on key terms
Consider the Arabic terms: اختراع (invention), صناعات (crafts), اكتساب (acquisition). How do they support the argument?
4
Apply the lesson
Think about how this argument strengthens tawheed by showing that all creation points to Allah's sole power and knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو كيف يكون الحيوان مستبداً بالإختراع ويصدر من العنكبوت والنحل وسائر الحيوانات من لطائف الصناعات ما يتحير فيه عقول ذوي الألباب ؟ فكيف انفردت هي باختراعها دون رب الأرباب وهي غير عالمة بتفصيل ما يصدر منها من الإكتساب ؟ هيهات هيهات : ذلت المخلوقات، وتفرد بالملك والملكوت جبار الأرض والسموات
Or how can the animal be independent in invention, and there emanates from the spider, the bee, and all animals such delicate crafts that the minds of the intelligent are bewildered by them? How then have they alone produced them without the Lord of lords, while they are not knowledgeable of the details of what emanates from them of acquisition? Far, far [from that]! The creatures are humbled, and the Compeller of the earth and heavens alone possesses dominion and sovereignty.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage uses a reductio ad absurdum argument against the idea that creatures can independently create their own actions. The author asks: how can animals be the inventors of their intricate productions (like spider webs and beehives) when they have no knowledge of the details of what they do? Since they lack such knowledge, they cannot be the true creators. Therefore, the only possible creator is Allah, the Lord of lords. The exclamation 'haitahait' (which is a variant of 'haith haith' or 'هيهات هيهات' meaning 'far, far') emphasizes the impossibility of any creature sharing in creating. The passage concludes that all creatures are humbled and only Allah is the sovereign.

Key Terms
اختراع
صناعات
اكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Animals have intelligence to design their own crafts.
Correction: Ghazali argues that animals lack knowledge of the details of what they produce, so they cannot be independent creators; all creation points to Allah.

Checkpoint

A) They do not have hands
B) They are not knowledgeable of the details of what they produce
C) They are too small
D) They do not have free will

Key Takeaway
Ghazali's observation of animal crafts leads to the conclusion that all creation is humbled before Allah, who alone is the sovereign creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The spider and bee produce wonders without any knowledge of what they do. Think of the countless processes in your own body - digestion, circulation, thought - that happen without your awareness. Did you create these? Or are you, like the animals, a sign pointing to a Creator? How will you acknowledge this dependence today?
?
Ghazali's argument humbles every creature. Is there an area of your life where you pride yourself on your own creativity or independence? What would it mean to truly believe that 'the creatures are humbled, and the Compeller alone possesses dominion'?

Evil and Divine Purpose
Defining evil and proving its impossibility for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why evil (qabih) cannot be attributed to Allah, based on the absence of purpose in the divine essence.

Core Idea
Evil depends on purpose; Allah has no purpose, so no evil.

This lesson unpacks a crucial Ash'ari argument: to call something 'evil' (qabih) is always relative to a purpose. Since Allah is utterly self-sufficient and has no needs, nothing can be evil in relation to His purpose. Understanding this removes a major misconception about divine actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on evil
Read the Arabic passage and its translation focusing on the definition of evil.
Notice the example of killing being good/evil depending on whose purpose it serves.
2
Identify two definitions of evil
The passage examines two possible definitions: evil relative to God's purpose, and evil relative to another's purpose. See how each leads to a different conclusion.
3
Understand why the first definition is impossible
If evil is what goes against God's purpose, it is impossible because God has no purpose. Grasp why having no purpose makes evil inconceivable.
Also note that injustice (zulm) is similarly impossible because all property belongs to God.
4
Reflect on the second definition
If evil is what goes against another's purpose, then one cannot claim God cannot do that—it would be arbitrary wishful thinking.
The passage hints at a prior argument about the people of Hell as evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شخص ، حسناً عند غيره إذا وافق غرض أحدهما دون الآخر ، حتى يستقبح قتل الشخص أولياؤه ويستحسنه أعداؤه فإن أريد بالقبيح ما لا يوافق غرض الباري سبحانه فهو محال ، إذ لا غرض له ، فلا يتصور منه قبيح ، كما لا يتصور منه ظلم إذ لا يتصور منه التصرف في ملك الغير وان اريد بالقبيح ما لا يوافق غرض الغير فلم قلتم إن ذلك عليه محال ؟ وهل هذا إلا مجرد تشتهي يشهد بخلافه ما قد فرضناه من مخاصمة أهل النار؟ ثم الحكيم معنا العالم بحقائق الأشياء القادر على فعلها على وفق ارادته ، وهذا من أين يوجب رعاية الأصلح ، وأما الحكيم منا يراعي الأصلح نظراً
A person is considered good in the view of another if it agrees with the purpose of one without the other, such that the killing of a person is deemed evil by his guardians and good by his enemies. If by 'evil' is meant that which does not agree with the purpose of the Exalted Creator, then it is impossible, for He has no purpose, so no evil can be conceived from Him, just as no injustice can be conceived from Him, since no disposition of another's property can be conceived from Him. And if by 'evil' is meant that which does not agree with the purpose of another, then why do you say that is impossible for Him? Is this not mere wishful thinking, contradicted by what we have assumed of the disputation of the people of the Fire? Then the Wise (al-Hakim) with us is the One who knows the realities of things and is able to bring them about in accordance with His will. Where does this necessitate observing what is most beneficial? As for the wise among us, he observes what is most beneficial with consideration.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a two-part argument: first, a definition and proof regarding the impossibility of evil (al-qabih) for God; second, an explanation of the term al-Hakim (the Wise) as applied to God and humans. The author begins by noting that the judgment of good and evil is relative to purposes (al-aghrad): a thing is deemed evil if it goes against someone's purpose, and good if it aligns. For example, killing a person is considered evil by his family but good by his enemies. From this, two definitions of evil are examined. First, if evil is defined as what does not agree with God's purpose, then it is impossible (muhal) because God has no purpose (gharad). Since purpose implies need, and God is free of need, no such purpose exists. Therefore, no evil can be conceived from Him, just as no injustice (zulm) can be conceived, because injustice is the improper use of another's property, and all property belongs to God. Second, if evil is defined as what does not agree with the purpose of another being, then one cannot simply claim that God cannot do such a thing—this would be arbitrary (mujarrad tashahhi, wishful thinking), as shown by the example of the disputation of the people of Hell (which was discussed earlier). Then the author turns to the term al-Hakim. In our (Ash'ari) understanding, al-Hakim is the One who knows the realities of things and has power to bring them about according to His will. This knowledge and power do not necessitate that He must act for the 'most beneficial' (al-aslah). In contrast, a human being called wise observes what is most beneficial with consideration, i.e., he acts with a purpose in mind. Thus, the divine attribute of wisdom is qualitatively different from human wisdom.

Key Terms
القبيح
الغرض
الظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God can be said to do evil acts because they seem harmful or unjust from a human perspective.
Correction: Evil is defined relative to a purpose. Since God has no purpose, nothing He does can be evil. What humans perceive as harmful is not evil in relation to God.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for God to do what is evil (qabih) under the first definition?
A) Because God is all-good
B) Because God has no purpose
C) Because evil requires a victim
D) Because God only does what is just

Key Takeaway
Evil is a relational quality dependent on purpose; since Allah has no purpose, nothing He does can be evil.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a hardship, do you silently accuse God of acting without purpose, as if He made a mistake? How does this lesson expose that accusation as a misunderstanding of what 'evil' even means? Will you repent from that attitude right now?
?
If you truly believed that nothing God does can be evil because He has no need or purpose, how would your reaction to suffering change? Describe one specific way you will train yourself to think differently when calamity strikes.
?
You often wish that God would act according to your purposes, calling it 'good' when life fits your plans. But this lesson says God has no purposes at all. Does that make you uncomfortable? Why? And what does that discomfort reveal about your heart's attachment to your own will over His?

The Meaning of al-Hakim (The Wise)
Explaining the Ash'ari meaning of al-Hakim and refuting that it requires al-aslah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari definition of al-Hakim (the Wise) and why it does not imply that Allah must act for the most beneficial (al-aslah).

Core Idea
Divine wisdom is absolute knowledge and power, not human-like purposive reasoning.

Having established that God has no purposes, this lesson turns to the divine attribute of wisdom (hikma). The Ash'ari school defines al-Hakim as the One who knows the realities of things and can bring them about according to His will. This differs sharply from human wisdom, which involves seeking the most beneficial outcome. Understanding this distinction safeguards tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Hakim
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the definition of al-Hakim and the contrast with human wisdom.
2
Understand the Ash'ari definition
Al-Hakim is defined as the One who knows the realities of things and has power to create them according to His will. Notice what is not included: any notion of purpose or benefit.
The definition emphasizes knowledge and power, not deliberation.
3
Contrast with human wisdom
A wise human (al-hakim minna) is one who observes what is most beneficial (al-aslah) with consideration. This shows human wisdom is goal-oriented, while divine wisdom is not.
4
Refute the necessitation of al-aslah
The passage asks: 'Where does this [definition] necessitate observing what is most beneficial?' The implied answer is: nowhere. Divine wisdom does not require God to act for the best possible world.
This is a direct response to Mu'tazilite claims.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شخص ، حسناً عند غيره إذا وافق غرض أحدهما دون الآخر ، حتى يستقبح قتل الشخص أولياؤه ويستحسنه أعداؤه فإن أريد بالقبيح ما لا يوافق غرض الباري سبحانه فهو محال ، إذ لا غرض له ، فلا يتصور منه قبيح ، كما لا يتصور منه ظلم إذ لا يتصور منه التصرف في ملك الغير وان اريد بالقبيح ما لا يوافق غرض الغير فلم قلتم إن ذلك عليه محال ؟ وهل هذا إلا مجرد تشتهي يشهد بخلافه ما قد فرضناه من مخاصمة أهل النار؟ ثم الحكيم معنا العالم بحقائق الأشياء القادر على فعلها على وفق ارادته ، وهذا من أين يوجب رعاية الأصلح ، وأما الحكيم منا يراعي الأصلح نظراً

The author begins by noting that the judgment of good and evil is relative to purposes (al-aghrad): a thing is deemed evil if it goes against someone's purpose, and good if it aligns. Since purpose implies need, and God is free of need, no such purpose exists. Therefore, no evil can be conceived from Him, just as no injustice (zulm) can be conceived, because injustice is the improper use of another's property, and all property belongs to God. Second, if evil is defined as what does not agree with the purpose of another being, then one cannot simply claim that God cannot do such a thing—this would be arbitrary (mujarrad tashahhi, wishful thinking), as shown by the example of the disputation of the people of Hell (which was discussed earlier). Then the author turns to the term al-Hakim. In our (Ash'ari) understanding, al-Hakim is the One who knows the realities of things and has power to bring them about according to His will. This knowledge and power do not necessitate that He must act for the 'most beneficial' (al-aslah). In contrast, a human being called wise observes what is most beneficial with consideration, i.e., he acts with a purpose in mind. Thus, the divine attribute of wisdom is qualitatively different from human wisdom.

Key Terms
الحكيم
الأصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Allah's wisdom is like human wisdom and obliges Him to create the best possible world.
Correction: Allah's wisdom is His perfect knowledge and power, not a deliberative process that seeks benefits. He is not obligated to create what is most beneficial for creatures.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the term al-Hakim mean when applied to Allah?
A) The One who always chooses what is most beneficial
B) The One who knows realities and is able to bring them about according to His will
C) The One who acts with a purpose
D) The One who deliberates before acting

Key Takeaway
Allah is al-Hakim because of His perfect knowledge and power, not because He seeks benefits—human wisdom is different.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call Allah 'al-Hakim', do you genuinely submit to His decisions even when they feel unfair or harmful to you? What recent event tested your belief that His wisdom is beyond your understanding? How will you actively remind yourself of this truth tomorrow?
?
If God's wisdom does not require Him to choose the most beneficial option for you, how does that change your response when your prayers seem unanswered or your plans fail? Will you still trust Him as al-Hakim, or will you complain about what you perceive as a lack of wisdom? Make a firm intention to praise Him as al-Hakim in your next difficulty.

Obedience and Disobedience Equal for God
Ghazali argues that obedience and disobedience are equal for God, distinguished only by revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that for God, obedience and disobedience are inherently equal and only distinguished by divine revelation (Sharia).

Core Idea
God's transcendence means obedience and disobedience are equal until revelation says otherwise.

This passage addresses a deep theological point: how can we know what God wants? Ghazali asserts that reason alone cannot determine moral value for God; only His revealed law (Sharia) defines obedience and disobedience. He warns against projecting human feelings onto God, which leads to a degraded analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its literal translation to grasp Ghazali's concise argument.
Notice the rhetorical question and the sharp critique.
2
Understand the core claim
Ghazali states that obedience and disobedience are equal with respect to God—He has no natural inclination toward either.
This emphasizes God's transcendence beyond human preferences.
3
Identify the source of distinction
The distinction between obedience and disobedience is known only through Sharia, not reason or human analogy.
This is the central theological point.
4
Analyze the critique of analogy
Ghazali criticizes those who analogize from human feelings of pleasure or displeasure, which are based on natural temperament, not divine obligation.
The analogy is invalid because God is not subject to temperament.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends by dismissing the analogy as irrelevant to the real dispute.
This reinforces that Sharia is the sole arbiter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع أن الطاعة والمعصية في حقه يتساويان ، إذ ليس له إلى أحدهما ميل ولا به لأحدهما إختصاص ، وإنما عرف تمييز ذلك بالشرع ؟ ولقد ذل من أخذ هذا من المقايسة بين الخالق والمخلوق حيث يغرق بين الشكر والكفران لما له من الإرتياح والإهتزاز والتلذذ بأحدهما دون الآخر وغاية ما يقال فيه انه يرجع إلى ملاءمة الطبع وليس هذا محل النزاع
While obedience and disobedience are equal with respect to Him, for He has no inclination towards one nor any particular connection to either, rather discrimination between them is known only by the Sharia? And indeed degraded is he who takes this from analogy between the Creator and the creature, where he falls between gratitude and ingratitude because of the pleasure, excitement, and delight he has for one over the other. And the most that can be said about it is that it reduces to compatibility of nature, and this is not the place of dispute.
Source pages p. 210

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that reason obligates obedience or disobedience before revelation. It states that obedience and disobedience are equal in God's right because He has no natural inclination or special attachment to either; the distinction between them is known only through the Sharia (revelation). Then it criticizes those who analogize between the Creator and the creature, noting that in humans, gratitude and ingratitude involve pleasure and displeasure due to natural temperament (ملاءمة الطبع), but this is irrelevant to divine obligation. The passage concludes that the real issue lies elsewhere.

Key Terms

معصية
شرع
مقايسة
شكر
كفران
ملاءمة الطبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that human feelings of pleasure in obedience and displeasure in disobedience indicate that reason alone can know moral obligation for God.
Correction: The passage clarifies that human feelings are due to natural temperament and are irrelevant to divine obligation. Only revelation (Sharia) distinguishes obedience and disobedience.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for distinguishing obedience from disobedience?
A) Reason
B) Human temperament
C) The Sharia
D) Analogy between Creator and creature

Key Takeaway
For God, obedience and disobedience are equal; only Sharia distinguishes them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that your acts of worship are somehow pleasing to God in a human way—as if your obedience adds something to Him? How does Ghazali's assertion that obedience and disobedience are equal for God challenge that feeling, and what does that mean for how you approach your prayers today?
?
Think of a moment when you judged an action as morally right or wrong based on your own feelings rather than on a clear command from revelation. What was that situation, and how does this passage call you to submit your moral intuitions to the Sharia, even when they conflict?
?
If your salvation depends entirely on God's command and not on the inherent value of your deeds, then every moment you are either obeying or disobeying His command. In light of this, which area of your life are you most tempted to treat as morally neutral—something God doesn't care about—and will you now bring it under the authority of revelation?

The Two Worlds and the Danger of Claiming Union
Ghazali distinguishes the visible world from the unseen world and warns against false claims of union.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the world of shahada and the world of malakut, the subtle connection between them, and why claiming union is a serious error.

Core Idea
The physical and spiritual worlds are distinct yet intimately connected, but never united.

This lesson from Imam Ghazali introduces a foundational concept: the visible world (shahada) and the unseen world (malakut). Ghazali explains that the heart belongs to the unseen, while the limbs belong to the visible, and warns against two dangerous misunderstandings: denying the unseen or claiming union between the two. We will explore his poetic analogy that beautifully illustrates their relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let Ghazali's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compressed style and the poetic ending.
2
Identify the two worlds and their domains
Find how Ghazali defines the world of shahada and the world of malakut. Note that the heart belongs to malakut and the limbs to mulk.
This establishes the fundamental duality.
3
Understand the subtle connection
Ghazali speaks of the 'subtlety and delicacy' of the connection between the two worlds. Consider what this means for how the physical and spiritual interact.
4
Recognize the two errors
The passage mentions two wrong beliefs: claiming union (ittihad) and denying the unseen world entirely. Both are refuted by Ghazali's framework.
These errors arise from misunderstanding the connection.
5
Analyze the poetic analogy
Read the analogy about the glass and wine. Reflect on how it illustrates the distinction and connection without fusion.
The glass represents the physical, the wine the spiritual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عالم الشهادة المدرك بالحواس وبالملكوت عالم الغيب المدرك بنور البصيرة ، والقلب من عالم الملكوت ، والأعضاء وأعمالها من عالم الملك ولطف الإرتباط ودقته بين العالمين إنتهى إلى حد ظن بعض الناس إتحاد أحدهما بالآخر ، وظن آخرون أنه لا عالم إلا عالم الشهادة وهو هذه الأجسام المحسوسة ، ومن أدرك الأمرين وأدرك تعددهما ثم إرتباطهما عبر عنه فقال : رق الزجاج وراقت الخمر وتشابها فتشاكل الأمر فكأنما خمر ولا قدح وكأنما قدح ولا خمر
The world of shahada (witnessing) is perceived by the senses, and the world of malakut (dominion) is the unseen world perceived by the light of insight. The heart is from the world of malakut, and the limbs and their actions are from the world of mulk (kingdom). The subtlety and delicacy of the connection between the two worlds reached a point where some people imagined the union of one with the other, and others imagined that there is no world except the world of shahada, which is these sensible bodies. And whoever perceived both matters and perceived their duality and then their connection expressed it by saying: The glass became thin and the wine became clear, and they resembled each other, so the matter became similar, as if it were wine but no cup, and as if it were cup but no wine.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage classifies two distinct worlds: the world of shahada (the visible, sensory world) and the world of malakut (the unseen, spiritual world perceived by the inner eye of insight). Ghazali explains that the heart belongs to the world of malakut, while the limbs and their actions belong to the world of mulk (the physical kingdom). He then highlights the subtle and delicate connection between these two worlds. This subtlety has led to two opposite errors: some people imagined that the two worlds are united (ittihad), confusing the distinction; others denied the existence of the unseen world altogether, claiming that only the physical world exists. Ghazali then quotes a poetic analogy: when the glass becomes thin and the wine clear, they resemble each other so much that one might think there is only wine or only the cup, yet both remain distinct. This analogy illustrates the fine boundary between the two worlds and warns against the false claim of union. The passage is a clear refutation of both pantheistic union and materialist denial.

Key Terms
عالم الشهادة
عالم الملكوت
عالم الملك
البصيرة
الإرتباط
الإتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The spiritual and physical worlds are united (ittihad).
Correction: Ghazali teaches that they remain distinct despite a subtle connection; union is an error.

Checkpoint
According to Ghazali, what is the error of those who imagine union between the two worlds?
A) They are correct
B) They are mistaken
C) They are of the saints
D) They are the prophets

Key Takeaway
The physical and spiritual worlds are distinct but intimately connected, and neither pantheistic union nor materialist denial is correct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that your heart belongs to the unseen world and your limbs to the physical world. But when you go about your daily actions, do you ever consider that your physical movements are connected to a spiritual reality? What would change if you remembered that every act has a dimension beyond the visible?
?
The passage says the connection is so subtle that some people imagine union. Have you ever felt so close to God that you thought you had become one with Him? What does this lesson teach you about preserving the distinction between Creator and creation even in moments of intimacy?
?
Ghazali warns against those who deny the unseen world, seeing only physical bodies. Is there an area of your life where you live as if only what you can see and touch matters? What would it take for you to give the unseen world its proper weight in your decisions today?

beginner
4 min
The Two Worlds: Shahada and Malakut
Ghazali defines two worlds and critiques two misconceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the visible and unseen worlds and the subtle connection that leads to common misconceptions.

Core Idea
Two worlds, one connection: the heart links the seen and the unseen.

In this passage, Ghazali lays the foundation for a proper understanding of reality. He defines two distinct realms—the world of the senses and the world of the unseen—and warns against two common errors: either merging them or denying the unseen. This lesson will clarify the distinction and the delicate relationship between them.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice that Ghazali uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two worlds and their perception
Look for the definitions of shahada and malakut. Note how each is perceived: senses vs. light of insight.
3
Locate the heart and limbs
The passage assigns the heart to malakut and limbs to mulk (part of shahada). Understand how this links the two worlds.
4
Understand the two misconceptions
Identify the two errors: thinking the worlds are united, and denying the unseen world altogether. Reflect on why these errors arise from the subtle connection.
5
Reflect on the poetic analogy
Analyze the glass and wine analogy. What does it teach about the relationship? Why might someone be confused?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عالم الشهادة المدرك بالحواس وبالملكوت عالم الغيب المدرك بنور البصيرة، والقلب من عالم الملكوت، والأعضاء وأعمالها من عالم الملك ولطف الإرتباط ودقته بين العالمين إنتهى إلى حد ظن بعض الناس إتحاد أحدهما بالآخر، وظن آخرون أنه لا عالم إلا عالم الشهادة وهو هذه الأجسام المحسوسة، ومن أدرك الأمرين وأدرك تعددهما ثم إرتباطهما عبر عنه فقال، رق الزجاج وراقت الخمر وتشابها فتشاكل الأمر فكأنما خمر ولا قدح وكأنما قدح ولا خمر
The world of witnessing (shahada) perceived by the senses, and the world of sovereignty (malakut) is the world of the unseen perceived by the light of insight. The heart is from the world of malakut, while the limbs and their actions are from the world of dominion (mulk). The subtlety and precision of connection between the two worlds reached a point where some people thought one is united with the other, and others thought there is no world except the world of shahada, which is these sensible bodies. One who comprehends both matters and comprehends their duality then their connection expresses it by saying: The glass became thin and the wine became clear, and they resembled each other, so the matter became ambiguous—as if wine and no goblet, and as if goblet and no wine.
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and argumentative passage. Imam al-Ghazali first defines two worlds: the world of shahada (visible, perceived by senses) and the world of malakut (unseen, perceived by the light of inner sight). He then locates the heart in the malakut world and the limbs and their actions in the mulk world. He highlights the subtle connection between these two realms. This connection is so delicate that some mistakenly claim they are one (union), while others deny the unseen world altogether, reducing reality to mere physical bodies. Those who grasp both and their distinctness, then perceive their connection, express it through a poetic analogy: when glass is thin and wine clear, they resemble each other, leading to confusion—one may say 'it is wine without a glass' or 'a glass without wine'. This illustrates the difficulty of distinguishing the two worlds yet affirms their distinct identities.

Key Terms
عالم الشهادة
عالم الملكوت
عالم الملك
الاتحاد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the two worlds are united (ittihad).
Correction: They are distinct but subtly connected.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do some people wrongly conclude about the two worlds?
A) That they are completely separate with no connection.
B) That one is united with the other.
C) That the heart is from the world of shahada.
D) That the world of malakut is perceived by the senses.

Key Takeaway
The worlds of shahada and malakut are distinct yet connected, and misunderstanding this connection leads to errors like denial of the unseen or belief in union.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if only the visible world is real? What would change in your daily life if you truly perceived the unseen world as equally real?
?
Your heart is from the world of malakut, but your limbs act in shahada. Are your limbs following your heart's connection to the unseen, or are they disconnected? How can you align them today?
?
The glass and wine analogy shows how close the two worlds appear. Have you ever experienced a moment where the divine seemed almost perceptible in the physical? How can you nurture that awareness starting now?
